included Brian Berman (Task Manager), F.rederick Hasselback (Financial Economist) and Peter Ianacbkov (Lawyer)
from the World Bank; and consultants Louis Carbonier (Natural Resource Management Specialist), Gene Elis
(Economist, FAO/CP), and Matfi Kirkkainen (Forestry Mechanization Specialist). Project preparation was carried
out by the Govermnent of Belarus preparation team included Valentin P. Zorin (Team Leder and Forestry Vice
Minister), Egor 1. Sharak (Project Manager), Mikhael L. Ambrazevich, Prof. Oleg A. Atroshchenko, Dr. Vladimir
F. Baginsky, Vladimir 1. Borodin, Prof. A. S. Fedorenchik, Valeri V. Gerasimyonoc, Dr. Victor A. Ipajtev,
Michael V. Kuzmenkov, and Ivan G. Mysleinko. Technical assistance for preparation was provided by the World
Bank, the FAO/CP and from the Consultant Trust Funds of the Canada, Dutch, Finnish and Swedish govermments.
Additional direct assistance for component design and preparation was obtained from the Danish and German
governments. Peer reviewers were Horst Wagner (AGRNR) and Francois Wenc6lius (EC2AW). The economic
analysis was reviewed by John Dixon (ENVPE) and Wiliam Magrath (AGRAP). The Division Chief is Geoffrey
Fox (EC4NR) and the Department Director is Basil Kavalsky (EC4DR).

LOAN AND PROJECT SUIMMARY
Borrower:           Republi of Bear
Beneficiaries:      Ministry of Forestry; Belarus S.M. Kirov Technical Institute; private sector
machinery manufactur.
Loan Amount:        US$ 41.9 million
-            Plan
IBRD      US$ 41.9 million
GoB/MinFor    US$ 10.8 million
Indtstry     US$ 2.0 million
Totdal Project C0-:  US$ 54.7 mMlon
Terms:              15 year term, including five years of grace, at standard IBRD variable interest
rate.
Project Objectives:   Forestry is one of the few exploitable natural resources in Belarus.
Therefore, GoB seeks to restructure this sector to ensure that it can make its
optimal contribution to the economy. The short-term objectives of the
project are to introduce significant sector policy and management reforms, to
re-erse the decline in wood harvesting and wood production levels, to
improve operating efficiency and cost-effectiveness, to substitute for wood
imports and possibly expand exports. The medium term objective is to
improve forest resource management and thereby improve the economic value
of the standing tree-stock within the framework of a market oriented sector.
Project Description: The Project entails: (a) Sector Policy Reform: to effect real price increases
in the stumpage fees, introduce open market pricing, liberalize both the
international trade and the domestic market for wood and wood products, and
prepare MinFor enterprises for future privatization; (b) Production Support:
implementation of intensified silviculture; creation of seed facilit!!s; provision
of harvesting equipment and spare parts; and introduction of efficient
harvesting practices; (c) Forest Fire Protection: provision of adequate
protection against forest fires and prevention of the spread of radionuclide
contamination through forest fires; (d) Forest Ecology: policy for the
preservation of biodiversity and species mix; monitoring of forestry air
pollution and radionuclide contamination; monitoring of fuelwood utilization;
couerpa   funds .rom Industi beneficaries of fte Prototype Fund



BELA RUS: Foresty Development Project
Loan and Project Summay
assessment of the ecological impact of the harvest and maintenance systems;
iproved MinFor regulatory fumnctions and enforcement; and planng of
wedand, drained wetlands and Chernobyl-contaminated forest resources; (e)
Instuion Building: professional training in forestry and business;
preparation for operations within an open market economy; forestry research;
planning of forestry higher education reforms; and, (t) Techical Assistance:
engieeng and testing of manurin prototypes; computerized plaming
system for forestry resource management; and upgrading forestry resource
planning. staff training, project management and audit.
Project Benefits:   The short-term benefits include increases in the amount and efficiency of
wood harvesting, cash flow generation from small wood sales, savings of
foreign excharge (wood import substitution and reduced -uel consumption),
and reduction of roundlog and sawnlog imports. The purchase of spare parts
and the implementation of new barvesting methods will arrest the output
decline of the last five years, preserve efficient manufactring and harvesting
job-places, and significantly reduce fuel consumption in havesting. The fire
protection component will reduce fire-related forestry losses and reduce the
risk of spreading the Chernobyl-radioactivity contamination through the smToke
plume. Medium- and long-term beefts include improved econonic value of
the standing trees, improved forest quality and tree health, and improved
forest ecology.
Project Ris
Risk External to
the Project:       (a) Macro-policy Reforms: potential for slow implementation of macro-
economic policy reforms could delay sector reforms, with a negative impact
on sector and project performance; (b) Sector policy Reform: potential for
insufficient reforms, and inadequate implementation of agreed and planned
policy reforms; (c) Indust   AdJustment: the wood processing industry
may inadequately adjust to real price increases for roundwood and fuel,
passing most of the cost increases on to consumers, and become uncomPetitive
with imports; (d) Market Demand: both the risk of higher product prices
and the general state of the macro-economy may result in overall dampened
demand for wood and wood products, thereby lessening project benefits and
cash flows from wood sales and stumpage; and, (e) Wood Markets: wood
industries may find themselves without adequate working capital to purchase
wood in the open market. This would dampen market demand for wood. As
a further result, the Wood Exchange may not function successfully during its
initial period, discouraging its initiators from pursuing its effective functoning
over the longer term.

REPUBLIC OF BELARUS
FORESTRY DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORI
PART I. INTRODUCTION
1.1          Belarus has both - rural economy and a culture where traditionally forests have played
a major role. Extensive forests have existed for centuries in Belarus and most have been used for their
timber and fuelwood, hunting, aid gathering of food and medicines. After almost total destruction during
World War II, the pre-war forested lands were replanted within the first three post-war decades.
Additional lands have been afforested since then, so that nearly 40% of Belarus' total land area is under
forest cover, 63% of which is used commercialy. As a direct result of this destruction and replanting,
only a very small portion of Belarus forest is currently 'mature" forest (i.e. over 80 years old).
1.2          Domestic demand for wood and wood products is currently depressed. Economic
recovery from the current transition to a market economy is expected to increase demand for wood in
excess of existing mature forest resources. Belarus requires a new forestry management plan which, in
the medium term, adjusts the forest resource management and its age structure for sustainable commercial
output. Its resourcer management problams have been compounded by the contamination of 20% of the
forest resources by the radioactive fallout from the Chernobyl accieent.
1.3          The breakup of the former Soviet Union (FSU) has placed significant strains on the
forestry sector and the wood industries by disrupting trade and contributing to the balance of payments
problems now facing Belarus. Traditional FSU markets are no longer easily accessible to Belarus' wood
industries. Mature sawlogs needed to maintain wood indstry operations at capacity are no longer being
imported from Russia, and machinery spare parts and replacements are no longer readily available.
Consequently, the forestry sector now faces significant declines in harvesting and processing capacity.
This capacity decline is more severe than the decline in domestic wood demand.
1.4          Against this background, the Government of Belarus (GoB) has requested assistance to
help finance projects both in forestry resource management (from IBRD) and to support the wood
processing industries (from the Bank and the European Bank for Reconstuction and Development -
EBRD). This Project was prepared between April and June 1993 by the Ministry of Forestry, with the
assistance of the Bank and FAOICP, using technical assistance funds from donor govermments.
Environmental reviews were conducted through November 1993. The Project was appraised in October
1993.



-2 -
PART II. THE FORESTRY SECTOR'
A. Forestry in the Economy
2.1          The forestry sector has a significant role in the economy of Belarus; in 1990 it accounted
for between 3 and 4% of GNP and employed an estimated 175,000 people. Total annual harvest of wood
is about 9 million m3 ob2, which is low in relation to the present growth of the forests (Ane 3). The
annual allowable cut (AAC)3 will increase dramatically over the coming two decades as the middle-aged
and marng4 forests reach fill maturity. Forestry is one of the few exploitable natural resowrces
available to the Belarus economy, and therefore could, with its future sustainable production potential.
play an increasingly important role in the Belarus economy.
2.2          Industnal logging and wood processing represented just over 5% of the industial output
and about 6% of the industrial employment of Belarus in 1990. Past trade in roundwood (los) and
processed wood was mainly with other republics of the FSU; wood exports were just 2.5% of total
exports in 1990, and were balanced by imports of roundwood and sawnlogs. Present wood industry
production is now mainly directed at the domestic market. The potenial to export processed woods t'
Western Europe and Scandinavia, trading on Belarus' present advantage as a low cost producer, has not
yet been developed. Poor product quality, poor marketing, and transport disadvatges, are the primazy
constraints.
2.3          All of Belarus' industries were highly integrated in the industrial stmcture of the FSU ad
have been seriously affected by the disintegration of the USSR. The Government now faces the difficult
task of takig control of a complex system directed toward large scale production and achieving the
r;est ing, downsizing, and transition of the industrial complex to a market system. This transition
equally affects the wood processing industries, the supporting meclaiical industries for the maufacte
of forestry and wood industnes equipment, and the constrction sector (as a major user of wood
products).
2.4          Wood Energy. At about 4%, the share of the wood-based fiuel in national energy
production and consumption is presently quite small. Notwithstanding, the use of fuelwood (and peat-
fuels) is higher than most countries in the FSU. There is a significant demand for wood for heating,
mostly in rural areas, but also in urban centers. Present annual fuelwood consumption is an estmated

3.5 million m3. Consumption was expected to decrease due to the ongoing gasification programs in rural
areas. However. the recent changes in imported energy prices now enhances the economic viability of
fuelwood as a substitute for imported heating fuels. With relatively low levels of investment in adaptation
of heat-only-boilers, an increase in fuel-wood utilization for industrial heating could be achieved.
2.5          Non-wood Forest Products. Edible and non-edible forest products are only partally
exploited by the local market. Exports of such products are minimal. Hunting, mushrooms, berries,
honey, medicinal plants, and various saps and resins constitute the potential range of products. Natur-
based tourism in Belarus has good potental within eastern Europe and the FSU. The potential for
western European tourism has not been developed.

2.9          Resource Potental. The total soure: MinFor
standing stock of wood is now about I billion m3
or about 135 m3/ha. By comparison, stock was 90 m3/ha in 1970. The growth rate is presently estimated
at 3.6 m3/ba/year. This should allow for an AAC of up to 15 million m3 ob, given the need to build up
standing stock and taking into account that about 80% of the forested area is exploitable. According to
exisfing yield tables, Belarus forests will have an estimated growth potential of 5.9 m3/halyear from about
2005 to 2010. This should allow for a futre AAC of about 25 million m3 ob (by comparison with the
present harvest of about 9 million m3 ob anmually) (Amnex 3). About 40% of wood extracted from
forests is for industrial uses and the other 60% has potenal for pulpwood and firewood, as well as for
other traditidonal uses such as building and fencing poles.
2.10           aU       t:  Forests under the jurisdiction of the Ministry of Forestry (lor) -
84% of the total national forest cover - are adequately managed (considering MinFor's limited financial
resources) for sustained yields of wood and non-wood products and for environmental protection.
Conservative silvicultural methods, insufficient financial resources, and the lack of a market for
smalwood' are the factors that have contributed to over-dense immature stands. This density reduces
fiture yields and increases forest health and fire risks.
2.11         Post-harvest Renrai: Since the end of World War II, four million hectares have
been replanted by MInFor, about 30% with selected seedlings and the balance with whatever planting
material was available. Although difficult under Belarus' conditions, natural regeneration was achieved
on about 15% of the forest lands. About 300,000 ha of new forest have been established by afforestation
of abandoned agriculture land. To support this relafforestation effort, MinFor has developed seed supply
facilities (seed stands and seed orchards). MinFor also has investment plns for modernization of the
seed and seedlig services, and the contiuous introduction of selected superior genetic stock, which this
Project proposes to support (para. 3.30).

harvesting data for the late 1980's suggests over- source: MinFor

Belarus. Yet field evidence, and the Project appraisal team's modeling of the actual Belarus case, both

2.14          Logging in MinFor's forests is the responsibility of MinFor's rayon-level enterprises
(mainly for thinning) and Bellesprom's logging enterprises (for final clear cuts only). Logging operations
ar-  mechanized and mostly carred out under the traditional stemwood method10. Most of the logging
equipment is of Russian origin, frequently more adapted to Siberian than to Belarus conditions, mostly
outdated, and environmentally unsuitable. MinFor and Bdlesprom have started using the assortment
method"1 for about 10% of their total harvest and have acquired a limited number of harvesters and
forwarders. These modernization efforts are stalled now as a result of capital shortages for equipmn
investment.
2.15          Wood Processing. Belarus has an important, but fragmented, wood processing sub-sector
which was designed to fit USSR industrial priorities. The main operator in wood industnes is

2.16         The wood processing industries are ill-adapted to present market opportunities and they
are ill-equipped to process the present and potential smnallwood dominated output from Belarus' forests;
even less so to search for and exploit export opportnities to non-FSU countries. The wood industries
used to process about 7 million m3 annually, of which 6.5 million m3 was produced locally and 0.5
million e3 (large diameter logs) was imported from Russia. Production in 1992 declined to 6.4 million
m3 (6 million m3 domestic roundwood and 0.4 million m3 imported rommdwood and sawnlogs) - a 7%
average annual rate of decline since 1989.
2.17         Processing Sector Constraints. The wood processing sector faces three nin problems:
*     The most efficient processing facilities (i.e. those of Bellesprom) provide primary
processing for only 35% of present annual forest harvests (Box 2); the other 65% is
processed in numerous small and generally inefficient sawmills. The result is a
significant waste of forest resources and generally poor quality of final products. An
effort to restrucure existing scattered sawmilling capacities and to reduce raw material
wastage was initiated a few years before the break-up of the PSU, but it did not produce
any significant results.
*     Existing forest resources are under-utilized since the wood processing industry has
insufficient capacity to process smallwood. The pulp industry is weak and mechanical
12.   This sub-sector is cuntly under detailed review by the EBRD for potential investmens. GoB is actively
planming the privatization of each of these enterprises, and 50% have been identified for the near-term.

wood-processing industries are not equipped for smallwood. Two MDF13 plants are now
under consideration for financing through the proposed EBRD investnents, which would
utilize some of the smallwood volume as well as wood-processing wastes.
*      The sector operates with ageing equipment and obsolete technologies, and with high rates
if labor, raw material and energy consumption. This results in waste of forest and
energy resources, substandard product quality resulting in difficulties in exporting and
mediocre economic efficiency of the sector.
2.18          One of the most significant constraints to the sector's development is the absence of an
open, competitive local market. Bellesprom operates as a virtual monopoly; its individual enterprises
need to be restructured into smaller, competing entities. To date only four enterprises have been
privatized. GoB agreed under the Bank's Re itation L1o4 to identify priority industries for
privatization; the Beesprom enterprises are among the top priorities.
D. Forestry and the Environment
2.19          Biodiversty  and  Nature Conservation. "   Biodiversity  in  Belarus has high
national and inerational significance, since forests and associated wetlands (Box 3) are habitats of rich
and diversified flora and fauna. In addition to species diversity, Belarus also has a variety of unique
ecosystems because of its location at the juncture of the western and eastern European ecological zones,
The Belarus wetlands lie in the Polessie lowland area (in the Brest, Minsk and Gomel oblasts) dominated
by the Pripyat River basin (Map 1). This lowland has a total area of 8.8 million ha (42% of the total area
of the Republic) with wetlands cover about 4 million ha. of the Poleksse. About 25% (1.6 million ha) of the
wetland is under forest. This wetlands forest is also 20% of the total forest lands of the Republic of Belarus.
About 26% (2.2 million ha) of the Polesske is covered by peat soils, with about 80-100,000 ha. of the peats
covered by forests.
Wetland Forest Species: The wetlands forest species mix is domnnated by broadleaves - soft (birch, alder,
poplar and willow) 75%, and hard (oak, ash, maple, hombeam) 15%, with some spruce 10% (mission
estimates). This contrasts with the national average conifer dominated species mix - spruce 11% and pine
58%, soft broadleaves 27% and hard broadleaves 4% (F1gure 2). The wetlands areas therefore support the
country's major concentration of the relatively scarce broadleaf species, estimated at 55% of total soft
broadleaves and 70% of total hard broadleaves.
Box 3: Belarus Wetlands and Wetland Forests

1992).

with consequent high value for its gene pool. Some of the last remnants of the primeval lowland mixed
deciduous forest of Europe are found in the west of the country. Given the strong cultural significance
of forests in Belarus, people still have a high degree of traditional knowledge about forests and forest
products which have all but disappeared in other European countries. Systematic efforts to record this
local knowledge, to codify and analyze it, are now urgent if this knowledge is not to disappear.

2.23          Forest fires are becoming an increasing concem of MinFor. During the 1980's, the
number of registered forest fires varied from about 800 to 4,800 a year with correspondg damaged
areas averaging about 2,800 hectares annually, ranging from a few hundred to the spectacular forest fires
(which affected about 20,000 ha) of the sunmner of 1992 as a result of an exceptional drought. These
1992 fires highlighted the inadequacy of the present forest fire protection resources and the need for
increased public education in fire prevention.'8

resulting from the damage to the Chernobyl mnclear reactor in 1986. About 20% of the national forest

2.25          The population in the areas with > 15 Ci/kma  contamination was resettled by GoB; in
areas with levels between 5-15 Ci/knm there has been voluntary resettlement. The evacuated areas were
assigned to MinFor for management, "rehabilitation" and fire protection. There are a mmber of forestry
issues (worker radiation protection and health, commercial exploitation of the contaminated timber, land
and forest resource maintnance and management, fire protection, etc.) that require resolution before
MinFor can manage these areas effectively. The incidence of human-caused forest fires in the evacuated
areas has increased significantly in the last few years. This increases the risk of contamination transfer
to neighboring oblasts in Belarus and other countries through the smoke plume, and the health risk to the
fire-fighters.
2.26          Perhaps the most significant impact of the contamination has been the psycho-social
impact both on the residents of the contaminated areas, and on the potential international buyers of
Belarus' agriculual and forestry products. Recent analysest indicates that the levels of accumulated
contamination in most of the wood are sufficiently low, by international radiation safety standards, that
they should be acceptable in international trade. However, the public and market perception of the
20.    Contaminaton is measured as Curies (Ci) per sfe  area - the Cuie is a measure of irradiation
transmitted from radionuclides. Radioacdvity exposure, or biological equivalent dose levels, are measured
in Sieverts (usualy milti-Sieverts per anum - mSv/a). The International Commission on Radiological
Protection has set nuclear-industry and radiological worker exposure maximum dosage at 20mSv annually.
A forest harvest worker continuously exposed year-round in the 15 Ci/km2 (of "3Cs) zone would receive
an annual dosage of 2mSv/a (AECL Research, 1993).

problem, notwithstanding the facts on the contamination levels, may give rise to market resistance to
Belarus' wood products.  Recent analysis of the exposure risks to workers in forestry and wood
industries2' also indicates that, with some prudent protection measures, workers face no discernible health
risk. Nevertheless the psychological effect of working in known contaminated areas requires that special
measures be taken to alleviate the worker's health fears.
2.27         Radiation has generally well documented effects on forest plants and animals, including
reduced growth and yield, loss of reproductive capacity and, in extreme cases, death. Among plants,
pine forests appear to be the most sensitive to radiation. In the 30 km zone surrounding the Chernobyl
plant (MNap 2), lethal effects and morphological changes have occunred in pmnes, but other species
(mainly beech, aspen, and oak) appear to have suffered almost no visible damage. Mushrooms, wild fruit
and berries, and game - which flourish in the evacuated area - have a high level of contamination; this
poses a serious threat to human health since these products are very popular in traditional cuisine.

management strategy in the contamiat  areas.
2.30         An impressive and comprehensive set of measures has been taken to cope with the effects
of Chernobyl. Those relevant to the agriculture and forestry sectors include: regulations to monitor and

prevent the movement and sale of contaminated foods; various agricultural countermeasures including
efforts to decontaminate soils; and technical measures to reduce radionuclide transfer from the soil to
crops and pastures. Measures related to forests include: cutting down and burying the so called "red
forest" which died from irradiation; afforestation of about 6,000 ha of the highly contaminated area;
development of preliminary and specific forest management rules according to area-specific radioactivity
levels; and establishing, within MinFor, a laboratory in charge of monitoring contamination levels in
forest products nation-wide, and developing security guidelines for forest work in contaminated areas.
2.31         While these measures in forestry are commendable, there is still an urgent need to develop
a better understanding of three major questions:
*     How can esng forests be pro,jerly protected and managed, and how can forest
products in conta  ed areas be best utilized? Methods and techniques need to be
devised and used for protection of forests from fire and disease, safe forest work, and
extraction of "clean" wood from contaminated areas.
*     To what extent should afforestation be used to mitigate the effects of radioacbve
contamikation of the soils? The respective merits of afforestation and other biological
methods need to be assessed; revegetation methods and techniques should also be
developed for the evacuated areas.
*     Can the economy afford to leave the contaminated forests - 20% of Its total forest
resource - under-utilized and under-maitained? Given the shortage in Belarus of
mature wood for both domestic demand and for potential exports, and the need in the
medium tenn to increase overall production from the now maturing stock.
F. Forestry Sector Institions
2.32         Forestry related institutions and organization are under pressure for three main reasons.
First, the disintegration of the Soviet Union has resulted in a significant transfer of responsibilities from
the cental union-level to the republics. Second, the transition to a market economy is having a negative
impact on the sector's institutions, and these are inadequately prepared for the demands of the new
economic system. Third, ongoing budgetary problems have reduced the scope of all forestry operations.
2.33         Mninstry of Forestry. MinFor is in charge of implementing national forest policy,
enforcing forestry legislation on all forest lands and managing 84% of the nation's forest lands. The
Ministry is decentralized; only about 40 staff forms a small central administration headed by a Minister
and two Deputy Ministers. The Ministry is assisted by six technical units (para. 2.36). Implementation
and local administration is through 6 provincial (oblst) services and 86 rayon-level forest enterprises.
This gives MinFor a strong and competent operational basis.



- 13 -
2.34          MinFor headquarters is structured in five Departnents or Sections:
(a)    The Technical and Scientific Department, with responsibility for forest exploitation and
wood processing, and the scientific and technological aspects of forestry;
(b)    The Forest and Hunting Department, in charge of forest management, silviculture, forest
protection, and hunting;
(c)    The Economic and Planning Department, responsible for overall planning and finances;
(d)    The Procurement and Investment Section, in charge of MinFor infrastructure and
equipment supply to forest enterprises; and
(e)    The Control and Accounts Section which has a technical inspection function, and keeps
the MinFor accounts.
2.35          MinFor has insufficient capacity to develop forest strategies and policies for the entire
forestry sector (this used to be located at higher centralized levels within the USSR), or to properly
regulate forestry related activities by contractors on its lands, and on forest lands of other owners, but
under MinFor's regulatory jurisdiction.
2.36          MinFor manages six organizations with technical assistance and operational support
functions in the forestry sector:
(a)    The Belarus State Enterprise for Forest Management ("Belgosles") (500 staff), prepares
the forestry resource management plans for all Belarus forests.' It used to work as a
center of excellence for the whole USSR, and 40% of its activities were for other
republics, mainly Russia. With the collapse of the USSR, Bdgoses is over-staffed to
serve the needs of Belarus alone. It has only limited financial resources, and is actively
seeking conuracts in the FSU.
(b)    The Belarus State Institute for Forestry Projets ("Delgiproles") (100 staff), designs
MinFor's infrastructure (forest roads, forest enterprise buildings), devises technical and
management regulations, and manages parks in urban centers. For the last two years it
has been responsible for the development and planning of superior tree selection, seed
procurement, the planning and design of seed facilities, seed quality control, and some
seedling production for genetically selected stock.25 It is also plans afforestation.
(c)    The Center for Scientific Organiation of Labor (40 staff), is responsible for improving
labor efficiency in MinFer's forest enterprises; it also manages the laboratory that
monitors radioactive contamination in forests.
24.   i.e. Including all the forests not under the management of MinPor (Box 1).
25.    Seedling production from local (mostly unselected) seed is also undertaken by eah rayon-level enterprise
for its own reforestation program.



- 14-
(d)    Two Technical Stations (20 staft), in charge of technical assistance to forest enterprises,
one for forest seeds and the other for forest protection respectively.
(e)    The Production and Sales Enterprise (20 staff), has operational procurement and trade
responsibilities, under the authority of MinFor's Procurement and Investment Section.
2.37         Foresty Manpower Training. Forestry training in Belarus has been to very high
scientific and technical standards. University training in forestry and wood processing is provided by the
Belarus S.M. Kirov Technological Institute in Minsk ("Kirov"), which was a center of excellence for
the whole of the USSR. Studies are organized according to Soviet standards, but the intention is to
reform the Institute to come closer to Western standards. The Institute suffers from decreasing budgetary
support and declining fees-revenue as student recruitment is now limited to Belarus. Total student
enrollment for the four and a half year Forestry Engineering course is about 450. Forestry technicians
are trained in the Polodsk Polytechnicum, dose to Vitebsk; 150 students graduate every year after a
three-year course of study. The Forestry School of Borlissov trains about 150 forest guards and
specialized workers per year, after two years of training.
2.38         Research  The Belarus Academy of Science Forestry Research Institute at Gomel
("Gomel") was a leading center for forestry research in the FSU. Research programs, until the breakup
of the FSU, had been very efficient, with a strong operational focus. However, research activities are
now threatened by reduced state budgets, which have forced severe cut-backs in the research programs.
Also, forestry research has not kept abreast with scientific developments at major national institutes
specializing in temperate forests research.
G. Setor Policy Issues
2.39         Sector policy issues fall into five major categories-
*     pricing and returns to the economy from the national forest resource;
*     replacement of the state order and product allocation system of the centrally planned
economy with market distribution;
*     the closed, non-competitive domestic wood and wood product markets, and tightly
regulated international trade in these products;
*     the lack of competitiveness in the wood processing industries; the virtual Bellesprom
enterprises' monopolies at the oblast- and rayon-levels, and the absence of entrepreneur-
based services for harvesting and wood; and
*     the need to privatize Bdlesprom's constituent enterprises and MinFor's rayon-level
enterprises.
2.4)         Wood Trade and Pricing. Production and trade of standing wood and lc:gs continues
to be centally planned and administered by Belcontract, a state trading company, and by local



- 1s -
authorities. Under GoB's plans presented in conjunction with the Rehabiltation Loan the State Order
system was to have been phased out by the end of 1993 (Annex 2, Attacdment 2). Trade of p,ocessed
wood product is now theoretically under a mixture of command and emerging market systems. But 90%
of MinFor production of roundwood and sawnwood is now being delivered under state orders and the
Belcontract administered centralized distribution system, to various state enterprises. The mindmal
remainder is available for trade on the open market. Pricing is determined by Belcontract and does not
reflect market conditions.

2.42         Policy Reform. During the processing of this  (Oct 1993) Soura: Mison srey
Project, and the accompanying dialogue between the Bank and
GoB, a program was formulated and tentatively agreed upon, for significant sector policy reforms that
address the issues listed below (Annex 2). These reforms represent a major first step to implement, at
the sector level, the economic reform program agreed by GoB with the IMFW  and with the Bank.'
2.43         The elements of the sector reform program were confirmed at Negotiations. They are:
(a)    Macro-policy Reforms applied to the specific case of the forestry sector:
(i)    phased elimination of the budget-subsidy element in the financing of MinFoes
production functions, and the implicit subsidy to the wood processing industries;
(ii)   liberalization of international trade in wood and wood products; and
(iii)  liberalization of the domestic wood and wood products market (specifically the
elimination of the state order system from the distribution of product and
administered prices);
(b)    Sector-specific Policy Reorms (mostly within MinFor's own operations) to:
(i)   increase private sector participation in the wood industry, and related services;
26.   Under the Systematic Transformaton faclit (STh approved by the IMF Board on July 28, 1993.

(ii)   establish competitive markets for wood, wood products and forestry services, and
the establishnment of a formal c2en wood market by MinFor;
(iii)   prepare MinFor enterprises for privatization; and
(iv)   preparation of a timetable for the privatization of the enterprises under the
Bellesprom association (paras 2.15 et seq.).
2.44          These policy reform measures are not without their economic and social costs. Market
liberalization could result in restructuring of the wood industry, with some downsizing and even closures
of enterprises, but with eventual increases in production efficiency and a market orientation for the
industries' products. This restructuring and adjustment may cause some transitional unemployment. The
liberalization of the wood market will increase the price of domestic fuelwood. Vulnerable social groups
could face hardships. The understandings reached with GoB under the Rehabiion Loan are to
address these, and similar, transitional issues through the job retraining and social safety net reforms,
without undue distortions to the macro- and sectoral-policy reforms or to the industrial restructuring
process.

PART m. THE PROJECT
A. Project Objectives
3.1          The short-term sector objective of the Project is to arrest the decline in wood harvesting
(see F re 3) and wood production levels, thereby supporting sector employment, limiting wood
imports, and expanding exports. Emphasis is placed on a "miimum package" of interventions to provide
these fast returns, supplemented by selected longer term interventions.
3.2           The medium-term objective of the Project is to improve forest resource management and
the economic value of the standing tree-stock. In addition, phased policy reforms for the sector would
contribute to the emergence of a market-based production system and introduce competitive pricing of
wood, wood products and services to the forestry sector.
3.3           Specifically the Project would:
(a)    support sector policy reforms;
(b)    arrest the decline in tiniber harvest, expanding anmnal production by about 1 million m3
ob of fuel- and pulp-wood from the present level of about 3.4 million m3 ob;
(c)   introduce improved forestry resource management;
(d)    reduce, and eventually eliminate, direct and indirect budget subsidies to both the forestry
and wood processing sectors;
(e)    promote the private sector in the forestry services and the wood industries;' and
(t)    begin the process of adjustment to the open market economy in MinFor's enterprises.
B. Project Description
3.4          The proposed Project components, summarized below, are:
Sector Policy Refonms
(a)   to increase the price to "first-takers"" for standing wood and harvested roundwood

C. Detalled Component Description
Sector Policy.30
3.5            During Project processing a sector economy refonn program (Annex 2) was designed
and discussed with MinFor, the Ministry of Finance (MinFin), and The Ministry of the Economy
(MinEcon). At Negotiatons this program was confirmed by GoB, and agreement was reached that, by
May 1, 1995, an implementation timetable for these reforms will be established.
3.6          The proposed reforms represent a major first step in implementing, at the forestry-sector
level, the economic reform program agreed by GoB, the IMF and the Bank under the adjustment
programs. The elements of the sector reform program are:
(a)    macro-policy reforms established under the IMF's Systemic Transformation Facility
(STF), and the Bank's Rehiliaton Loan, and now applied to the specific case of the
forestry sector:
(i)   reduction of the budget-subsidy element in the financing of MinFor's production
operations, and in the consumer wood-products prices, by signifLint real
increases in the stumpage fees and introducing free market sales of forest
product; allocation of all stumpage and sales revenues to MinFor;
(ii)   restructuring the relationship between MinFin and MinFor to reflect the profit
generating potential of MinFor's concessionaire rights over national assets, and
therefore to introduce corporate governance and accountability in MinFor's
management, such that profits so generated are available to the national treasury
either for re-investment in forestry or investment in other national priorities;
(iii)  removal of all export and import licensing, export taxation on goods not under
administered prices, and other restrictions on wood and wood products
international trade;
(iv)   domestic wood and wood products market liberalization through:
-     removal of the State order system and product allocation for wood and
wood products, except for those products covered under bi-lateral trade
agreements, and product required for GoB's own use (para. 3.15);
-     removal of all price and profit controls;
30.    There are no costs associated with this Project component: implemtation is within the capacity of current
GoB staff.



- 21 -
(b)   sector-spedfic policy reforms, mostly within MinFor operations:
(i)   increasing competitive private sector contractual services to MinFor (initially in
harvesting and wood transport), remove Bellesprom's virtual monopoly
rationalizing the size and scope of its associated enterprises, and reduce the level
of MinFor force-account activities in forestry operations;
(ii)   establishing competitive markets for wood through the introduction of roundwood
(i.e cut wood) markets at roadside, and the phased introduction of a formal wood
marketing facility.
3.7          The reform measures imply a significant change in the relationship between MinFor,
MinFor's rayon-level enterprises, and Bellesprom. This will lay the basis for (i) establishing roadside
markets (operated by MinFor), (ii) the introduction of MbFor's proposed national and regional wood
market facilities (para. 3.16), and (iii) the entry of new entrepreneurs into wood processing, harvesting
contractors and wood transport companies.
3.8          To date, MinFor has allocated annually to Bellesprom all the mature timber stands for
clearcut harvest and processing. These reforms imply that standing timber, in the future, will be
harvested for MinFor's account by harvesting contractors, and that the cut wood will then be sold on the
open market to processors. Therefore, in the future, both Belesprom and MinFor's rayon-level
enterprises can no longer assume (i) assured rights to harvesting areas, or (ii) assured supplies of low cost
wood. They will be competing both with each other, and with newly emerging private sector wood
harvesters and processors, for harvesting contracts and wood supplies.
3.9          The Memorandmn of Economic Reform Policies presented by GoB to the Bank in
conjunction with the Rehabilitaton Loan provides a timetable for the general reforms critical to this
project (para. 3.6(a)). A number of these reforms were to have been fully implemented by the end of
1993 (Box 6). Clarifications were received at Negotiations that the reforms relevant to the forestry and
wood industry sectors are being implemented, albeit more gradually than originally planned, and in a
manner appropriate for the forestry sector and the wood industries.
3.10         The pace of the introduction and effectiveness of these proposed sectoral policy reforms
will depend on the progress of the reforms agreed under the STF and Rehabilitation Loan, and the
recently signed Monetary Union Accord with Russia. The forestry sector reforms require that the broad-
scale general economic reform agreements with the IMF and the Bank now be translated, through in-
depth restructuring and policy implementation, into a series of both general and sector-specific reforms
in regulations and procedures (see Concordance - Annex 2). Agreements were reached at Negotiations
on a number of complementary reforms critical to the success of this Project, for implementation during
the Project (para. 3.12 et seq.).
3.11         The IMF and the Bank have recognized that GoB is moving cautiously along the agreed
path of reform. For this reason, forestry sector-specific reforms can be expected to lag behind
macroeconomic reform, deregulation and restructuring, by at least six months to a year. Consequently,
agreement was reached at Negotiations for annual updating, by May 1 of each year, of the sector policy
reform implementation timetable for the following twelve monfts (based on the results of the previously
implemented reform measures), in coordination with and in a manner acceptable to the Bank.

Source: Belarus Rehabiltaion Loan (Report P46147-BY)
3.12         Internatonal Trade lberalzation. To ensure the effectiveness of GoB's intended
international trade reforms for wood and wood products, agreement was reached at Negotiations that, by
December 31, 1994, GoB would issue relevant decrees and regulations to further facilitate the
liberalization of international trade in wood and wood products in a manner agreed between GoB and the
Bank. In addition, at Negotiations GoB recognized the necessity to deepen the implemention of the
liberalization of international trade reforms through facilitation measures such as the simplification of
trade and trade finance documentation. GoB pledged its cooperation with the Bank in studying these
questions during the preparation of the proposed Private Enterprise Development Loan, and agreed to
consider simplification measures once the results of this study have been reviewed.
3.13         Domestic Market libe on. The proposed liberalization of both the domestic
market (paras. 3.6(a)(iv) and 3.6(b)(ii)) the international trade for wood (paras. 3.6(a)(iii) and 3.12) will
give direct access to local timber markets to both new local buyers and foreign buyers, thereby increasing
price competition and internationalizing the price of wood. This will permit MinFor, and consequently
the national treasury, to capture the full economic value of the nation's timber, rather than only the
arificially low value now established through the stumpage fees.



- 23 -
3.14         At Negotiations GoB assured the Bank that access to the domestic market by non-residents
was not in any way restricted and that such buyers did not face discriminatory taxation. GoB also assured
the Bank that such non-resident buyers are free to export their purchasers, provided they have satisfied
the foreign exchange control requirements. However, to ensure this policy impact GoB satisfied the
Bank, as a condition for Board presentation, on the implementation status of deregulation and decontrol
of wood and wood product prices, as planned under the Rehabilitation Loan (Box 6).
3.15         The wood sector distribution of products was dominated by the allocation process under
the State Order system. Under the proposed program presented by GoB under the RehabDiltation Loan
this system was to have been abolished by the end of 1993, except for products conmiitted under bi-lateral
trade agreements, and that product for "the Government's own needs." Agreement was reached at
Negotiations that by Decetnber 31, 1994, relevant decrees and regulations, agreed between GoB and the
Bank, would be issued by GoB for the further liberalization of the domestic wood market in wood and
wood products, except for procurement of such products required under the State Order system. GoB
reconfirmed at Negotiations that the application of the new State Procurement System (as agreed under
the Rehabilitation Loa) would result in wood and wood product procurement by the State at market
prices.
3.16         Formal Wood Markets. MinFor has proposed a "trading facility" for wood, wood
products and related services. MinFor proposes to utilize this market for open bidding for harvest
contracts, sales of unharvested mature wood stands, and sales of roundwood and wood products.
Agreement was reached at Negotiations that the market facility would be established before the end of
1994, under Articles of Association, regulations and operating policies agreed between GoB and the Bank.
3.17         GoB taxes transactions on "exchanges" (i.e. Stock or Commodity). This may be
inconsistent with a liberalized domestic trade regime. An understanding was reached at Negotiations that
GoB would find an appropriate legal structure for the "market facility" under which such exchange taxes
would not be applicable.
3.18         The adoption of the open market for standing wood and roundwood31 is based on the
State's ownership of the nation's wood resources and the State's rights of disposal at the best possible
price for the benefit of the national treasury. MinFor acts in this regard as the agent of the State.
However, in the past, either for administrative convenience, or as a misreading of the actual legal
position, local authorities have successfully claimed extractive royalty payments (the stumpage fee) from
their oblast's and rayon's mature wood harvests. This was used both by the USSR government and the
present government as a mechanism for the funding of local authorities. The new Forest Law, presently
before the Parliament, establishes the State's sole right to the nation's wood resources. An understanding
was reached at Negotiations that, after passage of this law, GoB will promulgate enabling regulations to
establish (i) MinFor as its agent for the market disposition of the State's wood resources under MinFor's
management, and (ii) the State's full rights of use of all funds so generated.
3.19         Stumpage and Royalties. Agreement was reached at Nejotiations on the establishment,
by October 31, 1994, of a stuwpage and royalty policy agreed between GoB and the Bank, for
implementation beginning Jamnary 1, 1995. The Bank confirmed that it considered a royalty fee of
31.   Placed on the market by MinFor.



- 24 -
between 2 and 5 % of gross annual MinFor wood sale revenues to be a reasonable level, that the royalty
fees should be payable to the national treasury only at the point of the first transfer of ownership fromn
MinFor, and that for those wood products on which a royalty fee is charged, no stumpage is payable and
visa versa.
3.20         Agreement was reached at Negotiations that the stunpage and royalty policy should not
affect direct budgetary support for selected MinFor's functions. Therefore, certain annual MinFor
costs32 were not expected to be recovered from the stumpage fee, and would continue to be funded from
the national budget. The Bank confirmed that the target theoretical total stumpage revenue would be that
amount required to fully recover the annual operating costs of MinFor's commercial silviculture
operations, if recovered from the theoretical annual total harvest.
3.21         Private Sector Development. GoB agreed at Negotiations to prepare by December 31,
1994, the simplification of requirements, regulations and practices for issuing business licenses for
forestry and wood industry enterprises and contractors.
3.22         Agreement was reached at Negotiations that a program and timetable, acceptable to the
Bank, would be presented by December 31, 1994, for the pre-privatization assistance by relevant GoB
agencies for the commercialization and eventual privatization of the wood processing sector (i.e.,
including those wood industries now under the Bellesprom association). This would include the
determination by GoB of the applicability of the proposed voucher system to the these enterprises, and
the application of GoB's new corporate governance standards to these enterprises.
3.23         Impact on MhiFor. Aside from the policy dialogue that the preparation and appraisal
of this Project has afforded, (and which will continue through supervision of Project implementation),
the Project would assist in the implementation of these sector reforms through TA (paras. 3.39, 3.48,
3.37 and 3.50) to MinFor for staff training, management of contracted harvesting, and for establishing
roadside wood markets and the formal wood marketing facility. To effectively implement these agreed
reforms, MinFor staff would also require training in market-based business practices (e.g. new
accounting and cost-accounting systems).
Improved Forest Productivity
3.24         Intensive Silviculture (US$16.1 million total: US$9.5 million operating costs, US$6.6
million harvesting machinery and equipment). The Belarus forests were planted at high sapling densities
(13,000/ha), in accordance with then accepted USSR practices. Inadequate financial resources for early
year forestry maintenance, in conjunction with a past philosophy regarding forestry early-years
maintenance, has now resulted in standing timber with high densities of undersized trees, especially in
the 20 to 40 year age class. The intensive silviculture practices being introduced under this project would
concentrate on maintenance activities in this age class - thinning of the stands from about 6,300
32.   Recovery of all operating and investment costs for maintenance, harvesting and replanting of commercial
timber stands would be expected. Costs not to be recovered, or only partially recovered, would be those
legitimate "national" expenditures such as emergency and disaster expenditures (fires, floods, Chernobyl
amelioration, etc.), afforestation of new lands, conservation and protection, etc.



- 25 -
stems/ha at age 20 to about 1,450 stems/ha by age 40 - to provide space and light for vigorous growth
of the remaining trees.
3.25         The Project would finance a portion of the local cose of the incremental intensive
thinning program on 230,000 ha over a three year implementation period (1995 to 1997). The annual
program corresponds to about an average one-third increase over the current level of the annual thinning
program (220,000 ha per anum) of MinFor. The project-supported annual program is about 45% of the
intensive thinning that ahould, ideally, be conducted amually in the age 21- to 40-year class. This
program would result in an average additional annual production of about 1.2 million m3 ob of pulpwood
and smallwood, over 1993 levels, for industrial processing and fuelwood, for a total over the three year
period of about 3.5 million m3 ob.
3.26         The thinning program would be governed by new silviculture m_aitenance and
environment protection standards, that have been agreed upon between MioFor and the Bank. The
incremental equipment necessary for the intensification of silviculture would include: chain saws, adapted
light agricultural tractors, trailers with hydraulic cranes, field debarkers, modifications to trucks, worker
safety equipment and spare parts. The introduction of this mechanization system would change the
current heavy-machinery based mechanization system to a more flexible, labor intensive system with
lower unit costs.
3.27         A Project sub-component, the Pflot Prnming Program (US$70,000), would finance a
pilot/demonstration program of 5,000 ha of young forest (20 years old), and 5,000 ha of 40 year-old
forests, with the intention of testing the managerial and financial feasibility of producing knot-free
"clearboard" pine logs. This product has high market value in comparison with regular knotted pine
boards.
3.28         Harvest Mechanization Spare Parts (US$3.6 million) Output from the forestry sector
has been declining, due to lower domestic demand. reduced export opportunities within the FSU, and the
declining operative state of the harvest machinery pool. The average age of MinFor's machinery pool
was assessed by the Project appraisal mission to be seven years. Consequently, without immediate
investments n spare parts, machinery capacity would further decline, and forestry output would fall
below present demand levels. The Project would support investment in spare parts and machinery
refurbishing to reverse this trend. This investment would prolong the life of existing machinery for about
four years, at which point new investments would have to be made to replace the machinery pool with
the new light mechanization system introduced in the intensified silviculture component (para. 3.26).
3.29         MinFor will be continuing, with GoB funds, to harvest timber in the Chernobyl-
contaminated forests, but only in the lower-levels of contamination (i.e. in the < 15 Ci/lkm2 zones),
determined to be both safe for forest workers and that the resulting products would be safe for end-
users.33 Debarking and delimbing in the forest will prevent the transport of conUminated wastes to the
saw-mills. The Project therefore would finance necessary additional capacity in mobile debarkers and
33.   The Environment Assessment of the Chemobyl-effected forests (AECL Rearch, 1993 - Annex 1) has
confirmed the adequacy of GoB's radiation protection standards and controls for workers and for products,
and that they meet inteational requiremes. The EA suggests a range of stengteing measures (paras.
3.34 and 3.73).



- 26 -
wood-chippers (US$0.25 million), to reduce the transfer of contamnation into the sawmills and other
related activities (para. 3.34).
3.30         Seed Proc_sing and Storage Facilities (US$3.9 million). Most of the Belarus replanted
forests are from unselected seeds. MinFor has proposed an expanded progtam to upgrade the genetic
quality of replanted areas (i.e those not naturally regenerated). The Project would support the
establishment of 8 mt/pa capacity seed processing and storage facilities near Minsk, including two
regional satellites in the North and South-West, near the towns of Glubokoe and Gomel respectively.
3.31         The facilities would be used to process and store seed from selected species produced in
the existing seed orchards (13,000 ha), and selected seeds purchased from international suppliers. Belarus
supplies international markets, particularly with spruce seed (Picea excelsa), and these facilities would
enhance its seed quality for these markets. The Gomel satellite would also serve as the national
repository for the national silviculture gene-bank. A modern technology containerized seedling mursery
at the Minsk facility would be financed. This would be of semi-pilot size to gain experience for the
future modernization of the regional nurseries. This component will also provide TA to train staff in seed
processing and seedling production, and to plan the future superior stock seed tree stands.
Environment-related Components
3.32         Fire Protection (US$6.0 million). An improved fire protection program has been
developed which address the requiremnents for the radionuclide contaminated zones and addresses the
technological and management improvements in fire protection service nationwide. Investments include
mproved fire fighting equipment (fire engine refurbishing and refitting, new pumps, hoses, hand-tools
and water tanks for aerial fire-fighting); improved communications network; fire-fighter's protective
clothing against smoke and radioactivity, and personal radionuclide dosimeters for the fire-fighters.' Fire
management would be improved through the introduction of fire-information and forecasting systems,
integration with meteorological data, and manpower training.
3.33         Air pollution Monitoring (US$1.5 million).35 Belarus needs to improve the scientific
basis of forest air pollution monitoring so as to better diagnose the causes of defoliation, to prepare and
implement remedial actions for local source emissions, and to document evidence of transboundary air
pollution. Six field-installed "Forest Climate Centers" and a central laboratory are to be provided under
this Project. These are complementary to the ICP-Porest system (para. 2.22 footnote), and also to the
general Belanus air pollution monitoring system now being designed and implemented by MinNat. These
climate centers sample and monitor air, soil, water and foliage: the central laboratory would provide
sample analyses and maintain the database. TA for staff training would also be provided.
3.34         Radiation Monitonng Systems; Workers' and Public Health; Product Safety and
Quality Control (US$0.5 million). MinFor will be continuing, with its own financing, the harvesting

of mature timber, and the maintenance (through thinning programs) of young stands in the Chernobyl-
contminated zones of less than 15 Ci/km2. The Environmental Assessment conducted for the Project
(para. 3.73 and Annem 1) detennined that it would be appropriate to assist MinFor in the area of worker
safety and health, and product safety. This component would finance equipment and system development
to enhance MiNFor's existing forestry and wood processing workers' radiation-health monitoring system
in the Gomel and Mogliev obtasts. GoB's current standards and procedures ensure that Belarus' wood
products do not constitute a public health hazard. To tackle the public's misperception of the dangers
from the contamination of Belarus' wood products, the component will also assist in strengthening the
radioactive contamination monitoring and quality control of wood products through an international audit
and certification of Belarus wood output. This is intended to establish and build the international public's
confidence in the safety of Belarus' wood products.
3.35         Wetlands Forestry Monitoring, Protecion and Inventory (US$0.5 million). MInFor
conducts both commercial and protective forestry on reclaimed drained wetlands, in all the southern
oblast primarily in the Pripyat River Basin (Box 3 and Map 1). These forests are estimated to constitute
about 270,000 ha, 20% of all Belarus forest reserves. MinFor has recognized the necessity to change
harvesting practices, and strengthen planning, regulatory and monitoring functions of both MinFor and
MnNat, in the protected wetlands areas. This view is also reflected in the Environmental Review of the
forested wetlands (para. 3.70, and Annexes 1 and 7), which has also reconunended the establishment of
conservation areas, and the expansion of the ongoing inventory of the flora and fauna species of the
international important wetlands36 to include the areas of national importance. The completion of this
plan, fnanced under the project, will provide the monitoring program for the wetlands, their conservation
and protection, as well as regulations for forestry activities on wetlands a drained wetlands.
3.36         This component will therefore assist:
(a)   the completion of the species inventory on the wetlands of international importance and
its expansion to include the wetlands of national inportance and on the reclaimed
wetlands;
(b)    analysis of this data and the establishment of this database on a computerized geographic
information system;.
(c)   identification of wetlands areas of national importance for conservation, and preparatory
stdies for these conservation areas;

Tecnical Assistance and Institution Building
3.37         Forestry Strategic Plan (FSP) (US$2.0 million). To provide an adequate basis for the
future development of, and investment in, the forestry sector and wood industry, under the Project
support would be given for a Forestry Strategic Plan (FSP). There is a long list of strategic issues that
require careful, in-depth analysis that only such a master plan exercise can provide. This work would
be done with the assistance of international consultants. MinFor has determined that the FSP team would
initially address the short-term planning requirements for Project implementation activities. After he
initial planning is completed, the FSP team would then turn to the questions of medium-term sector
prospects (including market assessments of future opportunities for Belarus' wood and wood product),
resource management alternatives, the ecological assessments of proposed changes in forestry
management, research priorities, manpower planning, regulatory requirements, the future organization
structure of MinFor, pre-privatization work for the rayon-level enterprises, etc.
3.38         The Bank has indicated to GoB its interest, in principle, to follow this Project with a
second sector investment of wider scope based on progress made dunng the first Project. The Project
would, therefore, provide financing for project identification and preparation, based upon the national
forestry strategy. This project preparation would require TA to MinFor, which will be provided by the
FSP team.
3.39         Forestry Management Information Systm (FMIS) (US$2.6 million). Bedgosles (para.
2.36(a)) is responsible for the ten-year forest inventory, and the planning of annual forestry activity
(thinning and clear-cut) by stand. The Project would support the completion of the FMIS on the basis
of the system developed in a small-scale version and which has being substantially completed by Belgosl
with a local expert team from Kirov and the Minsk University Software Laboratory. The appraisal team
has judged this system as providing features that no commercial package has, and that it would be more
cost effective to expand and compete this system than to adapt a commercially available package. The
Project would also support the transfer of all forestry inventory data to a new FMIS on efficient, modern
personal computers. Further, it would permit the transfer of computer-based information to individual
enterprises, for the use of the oblast- and rayon-level staff.



- 29 -
3.40         Financing will be provided for computer hardware and peripherals (US$0.75 million) and
forestry inventory survey equipment (US$0.46 million); purchase of comunercial software (US$120,000);
and local expertise to expand and complete the development of software, with limited international
advisory services to provide the overall systems design and hardware specifications, and to define the
additional FMIS features (US$0.8 million); and for training of MinFor and other staff in the use of the
systems (US$0.5 million). The Project-funded computers and software would be available to MinFor and
to other major users such as Kirov, Gomel and the Belarus Academy of Sciences for the wetlands
inventory and monitoring system.
3.41         Forestry Research and Developmeut (US$3.1 million). Through the FSP (para. 3.37),
a review of research priorities and the development of a ten year research plan supportive of the national
forestry strategy would be undertaken. The FSP would identify medium- and long-term priorities for the
future forestry research program, and thus tb ! ationale for future investments in Gomel and Kirov. This
should be available within 18 to 24 months after commencement of the Project.
3.42         In the interim, critical priority research needs to be supported, including: the progress
and potential for amelioration of forest radionuclide contamination; the alternative forest management
systems under intensive and extensive maintenance; genetic tree selection, seed selection, quality
assurance, and storage.
3.43         Gomel, under thejurisdiction of the Belarus Academy of Sciences, is the one of two local
resources used by MinFor3 for scientific investigations directly related to forest management, for both
the short and medium term. Since the breakup of the FSU forest research, along with all other R&D,
has been significantly reduced through under-funding of all the Academy of Sciences activities. To obtain
the required research for its operations, MiuFor now needs to contract for research on an annual basis
for its specific requirements. The project would support the MinFor contract research programs at a
mber of institutions, including Gomel, Kirov and, for example, the state forestry research institutes
in Moscow and St. Petersburg.
3.44         Foresty Education and Manpower Planning (US$0.7 million). Training curricula and
programs for future foresters, and the retraining of present professionals, need revision to bring practices
up to date with European standards for temperate zone forests, and to reflect the new ecology-based
approaches to sustainable forestry management, biodiversity and forestry conservation. Future forestry
education will also need to prepare professional and technical staff to manage and develop the national
forests and wood industries within the constraints and opportunities of an open market economy. This
will require major revisions and expansion of the curriculum at Kirov, the Polodsk Polytechnicum, and
the Forestry School at Borissov. Curriculum revisions, expansions and additions are proposed for the
courses in Forest Management, Forest Biology and Biodiversity, Conservation Biology and Wildlife
Management, Forestry Economics, and Forest Industries Management are proposed.
3.45         The Project would support Kirov, the Polodsk Polytechnicum, and the Forestry School
at Borissov staffs in their efforts to redesign teaching programs, and revise the curricula. The Project
would also support related staff retrai-' 'i, through the funding of six weeks to three month educational
visits for faculty to institutions in wez tn and eastern Europe, as well as for curriculum consultation
38.   The second source being contract research to individual faculty members of Kirov.



- 30-
exchanges, Twinning arrangements with superior forestry higher education institutions are now being
sought by Kirov. This would deepen the professional content of the education refiorm and planning
exercise. An information access program is planned for Kirov, including foreign language training (para.
3.50). The Project would also fund minimat additional teaching and laboratory equipment.
3.46         It will be necessary to establish a medium-term Forestry Education Strategic Plan (to
complement the overall FSP). This would be the task of the team of the senior faculty from Kirov, the
Polodsk Polytechnicum, and the Forestry School at Borissov with some international TA funded under
the Project. For this plan, a forestry manpower demand forecast would be undertaken with the
collaboration of the consultants under the FSP.
3.47         Prototype Development and Testing (US$4.0 million). Concern has been expressed by
GoB that the use of loan proceeds only for imported forestry equipment would not support local
manufacturing, particularly for the conversion from military to civilian products. The Project therefore
would establish a Prototype Fund, under the administration of MinFor, to participate with local
industries in prototype development and testing for forestry and related equipment that is internationally
competitive. Belarus has major metalworking industries and in the past was one of the primary sources
of tractors in the FSU. While these products do not fully meet internationally acceptable standards, there
are the necessary design and manufacturing skills within Kirov and the local metal industries. MinFor
can provide the technical performance standards (as can the ICB bidding documents). Some local
manufacturing partners have already been identified by MinFor. Consideration is also being given by
various industries to entering joint ventures or licensed manufacturing agreements for specific machinery
with foreign partners. The Prototype Fund could also participate with such partnerships to support the
design and tesding of their first products manufactured under license. To be eligible for support from the
Prototype Fund each development partnership would be required to provide matching funds for expenses
and equipment. Therefore the Prototype Fund would be established witi US$2.0 million capitalization
funded from this loan.
3.48         Training (US$1.0 million). This component would include financing for training for:
(a) private sector harvesting contractors in both the technology of harvesting, the required compliance
standards, and in small business management; (b) MinFor staff in new harvesting methods and standards;
(c) Minfor staff in preparation for privatization of MinFor's enterprises and their potential role as owners
of these enterprises; and (d) MinFor staff in their altered future role primarily as forestry resource
managers and regulators with emphasis on management training; and (e) the training for an improved
regulatory role (regulatory policy, oversight and enforcement) and for MinFor and MinNat staff at the
administrative and field levels.
3.49         Project Implementation Unit (US$0.6 million). TA will be financed for procurement
consultancy and PIU management systems advice. The size and complexity of the procurement, the
administration of Project funds, and the small size of the MinFor's headquarter's staff requires that TA,
office equipment and vehicles, be provided to the PIU, and to train MinFor's counterpart staff in the
PIU. The objective of this TA is to build local institutional capacity for successful Project implementation
and to provide a strengthened institutional basis within MinFor for a follow-up Project.
3.50         Other Technical Assitance (US$0.5 million) would be provided for international
advisory services for specialized topics in, for example, the introduction of an adequate cost accounting



- 31 -
system; the computerization of MinFor's rayon-level accounts; efficiency studies of the enterprises pre-
privatization; Project auditing; advice on the pending forestry legislation; and foreign language training
for MinFor, Gomel and Kirov Staff. Specific topics would be agreed annually during Project
implementation.

3.57         Procurement would be undertaken in the following manner:4
(a)     Machinery and Equipment (US$17.5 million). To the extent possible, all machinery
and equipment (each contract above US$200,000) would be procured under International
Competitive Bidding (ICB) procedures, with the exception of some items as discssed
below. To the extent practicable, invitations for bids would be grouped in sizeable bid
packages to enhance competition and benefit from economies of scale in bid prices.
Domestic preference would be applicable for locally manufactred goods (including those
manufactured under license) provided that local value added equals or exceeds 20% of
the manufacturing process and meets other requirements under Appendix 2 of the Bank's
Guidelines: Procuremet under IBR- Loans and MDA Credits (May 1992), using the
Bank's Standard Bidding Documents for Goods. Equipme procured through ICB would
account for about 80% percent of equipment purchases. Procement of the rem
20% percent of the equipment value would be in packages suitable for procedures other
than ICB. The exceptions to ICB procurement, including all componens with physical
and price contingencies, would be as follows:
(i)    Limited Interntonal Bidding (LIB) (US$1.7 million).  Proment of
specialized equipment for the following activities would follow LIB procedure
in agreement with the Bank: seed processing, seed storage and conanerized
seedling nurseries, specialized air pollution monitoring and analysis equipment,
laboratory equipment, and other goods which have a limited number of qualified
suppliers, and for related parts and supplies.
{ii)   Iternational Shopping (IS) (US$0.3 million). IS procedures would be used for
small amounts of equipment and materals (each contract below US$200,000)
where the costs of ICB or LIB would clearly outweigh possible price advantages.
Contracts would be awarded under IS procedures, based on the Bank's
procurement guidelines and accepted practices. At least three quotations would
have to be obtained from three countries.
(iii)   Local Shopping (LS) (US$0.6 million). Sundry items, not exceedig US$50,000
per package, would be procured through local shopping following procedures
acceptable to the Bank by comparing prices obtained from ftree differet
suppliers.
(iv)   Direct Contrang (DC) for laboratory equipment and computer software where
standardization with existing equipment and systems is essential, or where there
is only a single acceptable source of stvpply.

3.61         The Project's disbursement profile (Annex 8) does not conform to either the sector or
regional disbursement profiles.45 The primary reason for this is that the project is designed for a
relatively short-duration (5.5 years) implementation of a group of activities that fall well within the
implementation capacity of the individual organizations responsible (para. 4.1). The critical path
activities, procurement of equipment, will be covered with intensive pre-implementation planiing and
international consulting procurement assistance, all within the first six months of the project. The initial
implementation schedules for major project components are provided in Annex 7. These will be revised
by the PIU, before November 1, 1994, and the final plans v-ill be provided before May 1, 1995.
3.62         To facilitate Project execution, a Special Account equivalent to US$2 million, representing
about two months of reimbursable expenditures, would be opened in a foreign bank operating (through
a transit account) selected by MinFor with the approval of BNB, and in consultation with the Bank. An
initial deposit of US$0.5 million would be made, and the amount in the Special Account would be
increased to the ceiling of US$2 million at the Bank's discretion and in agreement with the PIU. MinFor
would obtain permission from BNB to operate this free foreign exchange account, to have unrestricted
rights of transfer of payments internationally, and exchange of currency at the official exchange rate
without retention. The special account would be replenished to the extent possible on a monthly basis
upon receipt of replenishment applications supported by documentation (as outlined in paras. 3.56 to
3.58). In addition, replenishment applications would be supported by statements showing transactions on
the special account. Agreements on these terms and conditions for operating the Special Account were
reached during Negotiations.
3.63         Disbursement requests would be fully documented except for expenditures under contracts
valued at less than US$100,000 equivalent, and for local training, and operating costs; in these cases
statements of expenditure (SoE) would be used. Applications for direct payment and special commitment
may be submitted provided they are for amounts equivalent to US$50,000 or larger. The Bank would not
honor direct payment requests below US$50,000. All documentation would be held in the PIU for review
by Bank supervision missions and verification by external auditors.
3.64         Operafting Cost Financing:  For the silviculture operations, financing the foreign
expenditure equivalent from the Bank loan, would be on the basis of agreed production standards and unit
costs for the various work activities. Bank disbursements would be based on an annual agreed work
program, and against actual certified production (measured in physical units, such as the area and volume
of thinning produced) at agreed unit cost rates. The first year's unit cost rate would be determined during
the Project start-up period by the PIU consultants and agreed with the Bank, as a condition for
disbursements against this category; these unit costs would be adjusted annually and subject to
confirmation at the annual audit. Evidence of physical performance and actual silviculture expenditures
(by the rayon-level enterprises) would be retained in the PIU and would be subject to annual audit.
3.65         For the various research contractors, and the Belarus Academy of Sciences (with IWRB
for wetlands activities), disbursement for operating costs would be on the basis of statements of
