G. Environmental Considerations
3.67          The Project has been designate as Environment Caregory B. Environment Assessments
and Reviews* were undertaken during Project preparation in four areas:
(a)    a general review of the forestry environmental aspects of the Project's interventions;
(b)    forest fires and the implications for the spread of radionuclides from the Chemobyl-
contaminated zone;
(c)    wetlands forests; and
(d)    Chemobyl impacted forestry resources.
46.    Environmental Assessments and Reviews available:
Belarus: Forest Fire Assessment and Protection Strategy (Canadian Interagency Porest Fire Centre,
Canada, 1993).
Belarus: Forest Development Project: Environment Review (Euroconsult, Netherlamds, 1993).
Belarus: Environmental Review of Forested Wetlands (Interational Waterfowl and Wetlands Research
Bureau, UK, in cooperation with Ministry of Environment, National Forest and Nature Agency, Denmatk,
1993).
Environmenal Assessment of Radiological Consequences for Forestry in Contaminated Areas of the
Republfc of Belarus (AECL Research, Canada, 1993).



- 39-
The EAs and ERs identified potential negative environment impacts, other issues and have recommended
amelioration activities (Annex 7).
3.68         The general forest enviromaental review indicated that the proposed interventions by
the Project (i.e., intensified silviculture procedures, the introduction of new mechanization methods, the
forestry seed development, and air-pollution monitoring) would all have a positive impact on the
environment. The review indicated that the environmental standards and procedures for intensified
silviculture and mechanization proposed under the Project are environmentally sound practices and
appropriate for Belarus conditions.
3.69         The review identified the negative en-vironmental impact of the current, inadequate
supervision of harvesting by MinFor (mostly of BeUesprom enterprises and MinFor's own rayon-level
enterprises) and the lack of enforcement of harvesting regulations. Agreements was reached at
Negotiations that within three months after the completion of the FSP (i.e. at about the mid-term of the
project), GoB would present to the Bank its plan for MinFor's strengthened role in regulation and
enforcement for forestry practices and environmental protection, and GoB would present an
implementation plan for these improved regulatory practices for agreement between the Bank and GoB.
3.70         The forestry wetlands environmental review indicates some significant environmental
issues related to forestry on the wetlands and drained areas. IWRB3 has also identified the absence of
adequate oversight and regulation, of both harvesting contractors (a MinFor problem), and of the
Ministry of Energy's mining of peat soils for fuel (an issue for MinNat and the Ministry of Agriculture)
and the need to train regulators and area wardens. IWRB has recommended restricting all forestry
operations in these areas to the two or three months of the year when the wetlands are frozen over, so
as to minimize the soil damage from forestry vehicles. The Project's proposed light mechanization would
be acceptable in the wetlands, but not the current mechanization used by MinFor and Bellesprom.
Furthermore, in the transition zones to the wetlands, it will be necessary to introduce and enforce
regulations for building light temporary wooden fords over the creeks and streams that feed the wetlands
in order to prevent watercourse damage by the forestry and transport vehicles.
3.71         IWRB has pointed out that MinFor and Belgosles do not at present have special practices
and regulations for wetlands and drained wetlands silviculture and harvesting. One of the major
differences between dryland forestry practices and wetlands is the recommendation by IWRB that all
forest wastes be removed from the areas to prevent disturbing the wetlands nutrient balance with
excessive wood-sourced nutrients from decaying forest wastes.4'
3.72         Extensive peat areas have been, and are, mined for fuel by the Ministry of Energy in an
unregulated and destructive fashion.A These lands are subsequently returned to the Lands Authority in
the Ministry of Agriculture for rehabilitation. In general these are then assigned to MinFor for
afforestation. IWRB has noted Belarus' successful rehabilitation of some of these mined areas by
47.   In contrast to the recommended practice in dryland to leave the wastes on the forest floor to decay,
thereby permitting the recycling of the wood-based nutrients.
48.   IWRB has urged an interational Peatands Utilization Conference in Belarus, and is to seek international
donor support for this.



- 40 -
returning them to wetlands, instead of foresting these drained and mined-out wetlands. This has the
ecological advantages of pernitting the regeneration of the wetlands biodiversity. It encourages wildlife,
and could provide the basis for eco-tourism, provided that the areas uses are carefully regulated. Therefor
for mined areas, IWRB has strongly recommended their restoration to wetlands. If, nevertheless, mined
sites are to be afforested, then IWRB recommends against planting to a monoculture, but instead suggests
a deliberate planting of mixed stands of soft and hard broadleaves and some spruce. MinFor, under this
project has requested assistance in deveioping new management plans for afforested mined-out peat lands.
IWRB has recommended the establishment of a monitoring systems within MinNat and MinFor for the
wetlands in general, and the forested areas in particular, using the expanded national wetlands inventory49
as the baseline data.
3.73         The Chernobyl-impacted forests environment assessment proposed an operational
classification of the contaminated forests: (i) forests in the areas with less than 15 Ci/kma (the "lower
contamination zones") and; (ii) those in areas with more than 15 Ci/ka2 (the "higher contamination
zones'). For the former, forestry could continue with minimal investmnents to ensure worker safety. The
assessment noted that interim regulatory levels for radionuclide contamination for wood products and
exposure dosages for wood industry workers set by the USSR are consistent with international standards.
However, the exposure risk for harvest and processing workers needs to be monitored. The assessment
considered that, in principle, it is possible to continue forestry in the lower contaminated zones, while
ensuring and maintaining adequate protection for the workers and the public. However, effective
inmplementation of the regulations and monitoring procedures for the safe pursuit of these forestry
activities now requires investments in equipment and training, and the development of a monitoring
system.
3.74         The assessment noted the need to act with some urgency, even in the lower contamination
zone, to salvage mature wood, as there is a rapid rise in the contamination levels of the standing wood.
The assessment estimated that maximum (i.e. steady-state) wood contamination would be reached
between the years 1998 and 2010, decreasing only slowly over time thereafter (halving every 30 years).
Once steady-state contamination has occurred, at maturity (i.e. in at most 80 years time) the natural decay
will not have reduced contamination levels in the wood to acceptable commercial-use levels.
3.75         The assessment viewed forestry in the higher contaminated zones as a mitigation problem
rather than a comm.ercial activity, considering public safety and fire hazards. The assessment stressed
that in all contaminated forests the forest wastes are a potential environmental hazard. Saplings, tree
branches and undergrowth tend to be more contaminated than wood. Special waste disposal may
therefore be required. Ash from burning wood is also a problem as the radionuclides become
concentrated in the smoke and the ash. The assessment recommended additional investigations into waste
control in some geographic areas.
3.76         Envronment Assessment and Reviews during Project implementation: The FSP will
include Environmental Assessments (EAs) and Environmental Reviews (ERs) of forestry in the major eco-
49.   Now being complied by the Belarus Academy of Sciences in conjunction with IWRB, and to be expanded
with funding from this Project.
50.   i.e. Where the contamination in the soil is in balance with the contamination in the wood.



- 41 -
zones (dry lands, sandy soils, wetlands and peats, radiaton f".ests). Questions of sustainability for
commercial harvesting, environmentally appropriate age-class restucturng strategies, the appropriateness
of the present forestry protection and conservation categories and conditions, and the strategy for
sustained fuelwood production will be included.
3.77         Issues raised in the EAs and ERs conducted during Project preparation, and noted above,
will be considered in depth by the PSP (paras. 3.37). The FSP will also make recommendations for the
inclusion of environmental factors in the future planning process and forestry operations management.
Results and recommendations from the three reviews noted above have been incorporated into the draft
FSP Terms of Reference (ToR). Within the framework of the agreements reached at Negotiations on the
use of,the Bank's Guidelines for the selecdon of Project-financed consultancies, an understanding was
reached with GoB that the ToRs for the EAs and ERs will be conducted in conjunction with the FSP
agreed upon with the Bank by both MinFor and MinNat.

4.2          Sector policy reforms are at two levels: (a) national policy that has an impact on the
forestry sector; and (b) structural reform policy within the forestry sector.
4.3          For the national policy, the responsibility lies with MinFin and MnEcon.
Implementation of detailed aspects in the already agreed upon macro-economic policy reforms under the
IMF's STh, and the Bank's Rehabilitation Loan, are essential prior conditions for the sector-specific
application agreed under this Project.
4.4          While the overall strucal reform principles were agreed under the STF and the
Rebabilitation Loan, determiing the details of forestry sector specific reforms falls within the prerogative



-44 -
of the forestry section of MluEcon and with MinFor (with the agreement of MinEcon, and the
coordination of the wood processing industry). Implementation is the responsibility of MinFor. Timing
of implementation of specific sector reforms depends on prior progress of macro-economic policy
reforms (agreed under STF and the Rehabilitation Loan) which is the responsibility of MinFin and
MinEcon. A detailed sector policy reform program was agreed during Negotiations, together with GoB's
commitment to deliver a policy implementation timetable to the Bank by May 1, 1995.
Ministry of Forestry

planning, logistics and training activities. These include:

-issuing of equipment and machinery procurement documents, and completion of

completion of the preliminary and the detailed implementation plans (para. 4.6);

and spare parts needs, and the most suitable locations for the implementation of the

(e)   training of MinFor rayon-level staff and private contractors in new harvesting methods;

and Kirov (paras 4.21 and 4.23 et seq. respectively);

independent financial entity, placing the full allocation from this Project into its capital account, as it is
drawn down. It would administer the fund in perpetuity, and would reinvest its repayments, royalties
and profit participation in further prototype developments. Use of the fund would be restricted to
research, development and assembly of the first manufacturing prototype for forest and wood industries,
wood energy related equipment and machinery, and equipment for air pollution and radioactivity
monitoring and contamination amelioration.
4.27         MinFor would enter into specific agreements with each manufacturer, including the
agreement that the manufacturer invest in principle at least equally from its own resources for each



- 48 -
prototype developed from this fund.' Specifications for each proposed product would be agreed between
MinFor and the enterprises. MinFor would establish a Management Board for the Prototype Fund
consisting of appropriate external expertise. The appointments to this Board would be made for a period
of three years, and would be done in consultation with the Bank. MinFor would also establish a product-
development steering committee for each investment drawing on appropriate technical expertise from
academia and commercial expertise from the private sector.
4.28         If, at the conclusion of prototype testing, the decision is made by the industrial partner
to proceed with commercial manufacture, the enterprise would agree to repay to the Prototype Fund,
through a 3 % of annual gross sales revenues royalty charge from sales of the product and its subsequent
versions, for a period of 10 years. If after the prototype stage, the industrial partner does not move to
commercialization within 2 years, the prototype and all the technical drawings, the testing results, etc.,
become the possession of the Prototype Fund in lieu of repayment of the Fund's portion of the
development costs. The Fund may then permit another industrial partner to develop the conmnercial
product under similar royalty conditions.
4.29         These arrangements were agreed during Negotiations, including the preparation of the
Fund's Charter and bye-laws, for approval by the Bank no later than September 30, 1994, for formal
establishment by December 1, 1994. An understanding was reached at Negotiations with GoB that the
Fund's bye-laws would limit risk-exposure to US$100,000 for any one prototype, and that total Fund
exposure at any one time to any single manufacture (and its subsiliaries, partnerships and/or joint
ventures) would be limited to US$300,000.
4.30         Training. The training programs proposed for MinFor staff and for potential private
sector contractors would be administered by MinFor, using training contractors for the design and
implementation of the programs. MinFor would utilize a Training Advisory Panel including MinFor,
Bellesprom, the FSP consultants and the international advisory panel to Kirov. The responsibility for
course development and the training of local trainers and course development (by international experts)
would be determined on a case-by-ase basis by MinFor in consultation with the FSP consultants and the
international advisory panel to Kirov.
4.31         Training of the staff would be assigned to local trainers who had been trained under the

be submitted by MinFor for approval by the Bank by enid-June of each year.

B. Project Monitoring and Evaluation
4.33         One of the initial tasks of the FSP would be the development of a detailed implementation
program for the Project (para. 4.14) This would include time- and activity-based targets and perfornce
measures. In addition, externality indicators would be included (e.g. direct employment generation,
market and price development, etc.). The task of the project management consultants in the PIU would
be to assist MinFor to design the reporting systems, including the measurement of actual performance
against these targets, and to assure timely and quality reporting. The matrix would be transferred to the
oblast-and rayon-level implementors for reporting to the PIU and for self-evaluation.
4.34         Project evaluation and the responsibility for the PCR would rest with MinFor utilizing,
among others, the expertise and data of the PIU. MinFor's responsibility for the PCR was confirmed
at Negotiations. The evaluation of the silviculture, seed, air pollution and environmental aspects of the
Project should include independent assessments by experts appointed from Gomel, Kirov and by MInNat,
and that these evaluations be included in the PCR. For the education and R&D components, independent
assessments by the Ministry of Education and Bellesprom Academy of Sciences, would be required with
assistance from the international advisory panel (paras 4.21 and 4.24 respectively).
C. Accounts and Audits
4.35         Separate accounts for the Project's expenditures would be kept by the PIU and within the
general accounts of MinFor its rayon-level enterprises, the Protope Fund, Gomel, Kirov, and the
Belarus Academy of Sciences, and would subject to independent annual audit. As audit arrangements
in Belarus are rudimentary, agreement was reached at Negotiations that an accounting firm be hired under
the "other technical assistance" component for the annual audit (para. 4.32) with qualifications acceptable
to the Bank and GoB. Because the funding for most of the Project components would be integral with
daily operations of MinFor and Gomel, the PIU project management consultants would be required to
set up adequate accounts and procedures for all the Project implementors (para. 4.1), integrated as far
as is feasible with the accounting system.
4.36         For the intensified silviculture sub-component where loan financing would be based on
certified perfonnance, the PILl and FSP consultants would together set up the necessary performance
measurement and reporting, for each rayon-level enterprise. However, for reimbursement on this
expenditure item the PIU would establish an annual standard cost schedule, and reimbursement would
be on the basis of certified physical performance at the unit cost rates. Determination of the
reasonableness of the units cost schedule would be examined at the annual audit, as would the
measurement, reporting and certification process for physical performance.
4.37         Because of the dependency of disbursements for a number of components on audit results,
agreements were reached at Negotiations that the annual audit would be completed and the fnal annual



- 50-
audit report delivered by the external auditors, through the PIU to the Bank, no later than the end of
March of each year, for the previous year's accounts.
D. Implementation and Supervision Plan
4.38         Project Implementation. The PIU would implement the production components of the
Project through the rayon-level enterprises (paras 4.6 and 4.14 et seq.), with management assistance from

4.39         A preliminary indicative project inplementation schedule has been drawn up (Amex 7),

develop the anmnal detailed implementation plans for the intensive silviculture and mechanization
components.
4.40         The critical path for the implementation of the air pollution and radiation monitoring, fire
protection and the seeds facilities components is the procurement of equipment and, in the case of the
latter, the awarding of the contracts to build the facilities. The PIU's procurement advisors would assist
with the preparation of the procurement documentation, and the procurement process could be initiated
before the end of 1994.
4.41         Gomel, Kirov, the FMIS and wetlands teams will each have developed their plans for
the first year of their respective component implementation before Project effectiveness, and these wi1l
be submitted for approval to the PIU staff (whose appointment is a condition of effectiveness) and the
Bank.
4.42         Supevislon.  It is estimated that the Project would requwre higher than normal
supervisory coefficients especially in the initial 18 month period, with visits by Bank staff and
consultants, about three times a year. With the intention to follow this Project with a wider investment
in the forestry sector, supervision would be combined with project preparation tasks in the last year of
the Project. Detailed supervision plans would be based on the detailed annual implementation plans to
be presented annually to the Bank by the PIU, under agreement reached at Negotiations (see para. 4.5).
4.43         Mid-term Review. An understanding was reached at Negotiations on a Mid-term Review
(MTR) of the Project by a joint team of the Bank and MinFin, with Mlnor/PIU acting as its secretariat.
This review would be timed for approximately three montis after completion of the FSP, expected near
the end of the second year of project implementation. The expectation of the MTR is that GoB53 and
MinFor would, by that time, have conducted an in-depth review of the FSP and prepared the

PART V. PRODUCTION, MARKETS AND PRICING
A. Production
5.1          Projected harvest is shown in Table S below. The incremental thinning component of the
Project is expected to produce incremcntal pulpwood and fuelwood of about 0.9 to 1.3 million m3
amually from 1995 to 1997 by comparison with 1993 levels. The refurbishing of the harvesting
equipment would prevent further decline from 1993 harvest levels.  It is notable that production is
below the potential AAC (Annex 3), reflecting both the danmened market demand (see para. 5.4) and
the decline in the machine capacity for harvesting of mature wood.5 The slow development of the mature
wood volumes to reach the fill AAC by the end of the century reflects the mission's assessment of the
development of market demand and the availability of capital for the necessary investments in new
harvesting equipment.

5.2          Even though markets are currently depressed, the proposed level of investment in the
sector and level of timber extraction are necessary in order not to jeopardize the medium-term viability
of the existing forest (Amex 3). To mainain the commercial viability of the forest a minimum level of
extraction of undersized and mature trees must be undertaken, and plantations thinned to promote
vigorous growth of the remaining trees. This requires a minimm level of production, almost irrespective
of market demand conditions.

B. Markets
5.3          Market demand and future trends for exports and imports cannot be easily projected at
this time. International pulpwood markets are depressed. The FSU markets have collapsed. The future
impact of Russian production of pulpwood, pulp, and wood products is not known, as the Russian
forestry industry is yet to be restructured. It has vast standing wood resources and excess, albeit
mislocated, processing capacity.
5.4          The local market for wood and wood products is presently artificially depressed because
of the hardships of economic transition, and the consequent darnpening in consumer buying power.
MinFor reports growing inventories of thinnings (essentially fuelwood and pulpwood), and a reluctance
to harvest even more thinnings if no mnarket outlets can be found. Paradoxically there are signs of
increasing constraints to wood products supply (based however on mature logs) and indicators of
artificially depressed demand:
(a)   in the larger cities, an expansion in new private housing construction has created an on-
going demand for wooden construction elements, with complaints of significant shortages
of these construction components;
(b)    complaints are also being voiced about the long waiting period for furniture, in spite of
the perceived high price for furniture;
(c)   there is growing private sector interest in investing in new wood industries (often export
oriented); and
(d)    a vigorous informal market is now being reported in wood and wood products,
particularly in unprocessed large-sized roundlogs and construction elements.
5.5          Given the chronic shortage of housing in Belarus, there should be a significant expansion
in housing construction once the economic reforms take hold, with a positive impact on wood product
demand. Directly related to this expected expansioi in housing construction will be r.ew home-owner
spendinmg on furniture.
5.6          There is a domestic market for fuelwood. To date it has been artificially supported by
subsidized fuelwood prices, but at the same tine it has been artificially dampened by low conventional
energy costs. With the expected real increases in fuel costs, a switch to fuelwood could occur. There
could be a significant market impact (and consequent positive economic impact) if industrial use of
fuelwood was substituted for imported heating fuels. For this to be achieved, however, investments in
modifications55 to heat-only-boiler would be needed. GoB's strategy for expanded fuelwood utilization
is now being examined under the Bank's Belarus Energy Sector Review. Potential investments could be
funded from the planned loan to this sector. The mission estimates that potential demand for fuelwood,
as a substitute for fuel imports, could reach 2 million n3 ob annually.
55.    For wood-chip feeders and emissions controls, and for the burning of radioactive wood, safe ash disposal.



- 54 -
5.7           MinFor had expected to export pulpwood to Scandinavia but this has proven unprofitable
due to a recent three-fold increase in Russian rail rates. However, given the dynamics of the international
pulp market, future market corrections to international pulpwood prices will, despite the increased
Russian rail rates, resurrect export potential for Belarus pulpwood.5' Based on market surveys, MinFor
has an annual target of 1 million mr ob of pulpwood exports, a threefold increase over present export
levels.
5.8           Belams' main competitors in the pulpwood markets are the Baltic states and Russia, all
of which have advantages in transport costs to Scandinavia compared to Belarus. Belarus' current
comparative advantage lies in its currently low labor costs, which may be a reflection of current
exchange rate misalignments. The Project is tailored to exploit this present advantage of the country's
low-cost labor by emphasizing efficient labor inputs and reducing capital costs. Data available from the
EBRD's analysis of the smallwood-based manufacturing and investment potential, indicates that the
present margin of advantage for Belarus is large and that wage rates can increase in real terms very
significantly before this seriously impedes Belarus' cost advantage. As real wages can be expected to
increase over time, the Project's emphasis on improving market-efficiency both in harvesting and in
processing should compensate for unfavorable changes in factor costs, and ensure that Belarus' wood
industries establish and maintain a competitive edge.

5.11         The proposal by MinFor to open the Belarus market to international buyers and sellers
should cause local prices to move towards neighboring country prices, adjusted for transport costs and
quality. Currently, with the absence of an open market for standing wood, no market price trend can
be projected. However, a black market is reported based on the redistribution of allocated roundwood.
While prices in this market for roundwood were not accessible to the appraisal mission, it was understood
that they were many times the official stupage price.

PART VI. PROJECT BENEFITS AND RISKS
A. Benefits
6.1          The Project would provide financial, economic, social, environmental, and institutional
benefits. The most important benefits are the short- and medium-term promotion of economic efficiency
in the management and protection of the forests and in the long term increases in forest yield and the
extraction of the lumber. The quantifiable and non-quantifiable benefits for the Project are outlined
below.
Direct Economic Benefits
6.2          Improved economic returns would be achieved from the following specific forestry
management programs:
(a)    Thim_ns progam. Both short- and long-term benefits will be derived; short-term
benefits will include incremental pulpwood and fuelwood production, while long-tem
benefits include higher tree growth and productivity, improved stand health, improved
logging efficiency and therefore lower logging costs and fuel consumption, and a
reduction in fuel-oil imports. Production and consumption of fuelwood, particularly as
a substitute for oil and gas imports from Russia and coal imports, would provide
significant benefits in foreign exchange savings.
(b)   Modernization of harvesting operations.  The introduction of light harvesting
mechanization will reduce fuel and labor costs, and contribute to higher efficiency and
productivity, thus increasing economic returns and saving foreign exchange on reduced
fuel import requirements. Similarly the provision of spare parts for existing machinery
will, for the short-term, reduce operating costs.
(c)    Seed Facilties. The provision of selected seeds will improve the genetic quality for that
standing forest species selected for improved tree growth, thus reducing the costs of
replanting, maintenance, and harvesting. Improved seedlings would permit a 50%
reduction in the density of seedlings planted from the present 7,500/ha, to 3,500/ha.
This reduced planting density would, in turn, significantly reduce the expenditures on
thinning during the immature years as well as mature-stand harvest costs. For these
reasons, the forest industry expects significant operating cost savings from the
introduction of improved genetic material and improved seedling quality over the life of
a forest stand (80 to 100 years).
(d)   Employment. At a time of increasing under- and unemployment, the Project will
promote rural job preservation, and even job creation, resulting from expanded intensive
57.   The thinning could best be utilized if there is conversion of small boilers (heat and/or steam) that are
located in the vicinity of the fuel source.



- 57 -
silviculture and the refurbishing of the mechanized harvesting capacity. The Prototype
Fund will, in the short term, employ skilled R&D professionals and should evolve to
future manufacturing of forestry equipment, where significant skilled labor would be
required in the metal working industries (mostly military) that currently face difficulties
in the transition of the market economy.
(e)    Reduction in forest fires. The proposed investments in fire protection ate judged to
improve fire fighting capabilities such that they would reduce the average area bumed in
a fire by 50%. The benefits to the economy of areas that are saved from fire are larger
than just the commercial value of the timber saved (see pan. 6.11). In addition, direct
public health benefits accrue (to both Belarus and its neighbors5s) from reduced risk of
further spread of radionuclide contamination from fires in the Chernobyl-contaminated
forests.
(f)    FMIS. The use of the improved FMIS increases the efficiency of silviculture operations,
facilitating optimal choices of stands and timing of maintenance and harvest. Commercial
forestry operations expect about a 5 to 10% efficiency improvement and anmual operafing
costs savings from operative and plamning uses of FMIS.
Indirect Economic and Social Benefits
6.3           Instituon Building. Development of improved management, corporate planning and
marketing skills, coupled with use of the FMIS, will increase efficiency and improve the decision
management process vithin MinFor and its enterprises. Investments in education and research will
improve the skills of the work force and also increase the stock of applied technology available to
forestry.
6.4           Stnctural adjustments. Market efficiencies wiRl be promoted with (a) real increses in
stumpage fee rates; (b) reallocation of stumpage revenues directly to MbFor; (c) the reduction of GoB
fnding of MinFor's production and maintenance operations;59 (d) free market pricing for wood and
wood products; and (e) the establishment of competition for wood supplies and forest services will
encourage entrepreneurship and competition. Establishment of a Wood Exchange (as now proposed by
MinFor) would facilitate this process. Wood prices should then approach 'efficiency pricing" levels,
contributing to price stabilization at realistic levels.
58.    See footnote 62.
59.    GOB budget funding is expected to continue for national priorities and social investments such affonsion
of marginal lands, fire and flood protection and post-iater rehabilitation; forest, forest-land and
watershed conservation and protection functions; Chernobyl-related amelioration activities; conservation
functions; etc.

strain on MinFin as it no longer has the resources which to increase MinFor's budget.

6.6          The Project. MinFin is expected to contribute to a significant portion of local costs of
the Project. Given the lack of GoB budgetary resources, the Project proposes the following policy
reforms to improve cash flow: (a) GoB will increase stumpage fees in real terms over a three year period
to reach full cost recovery of MinFor's total silviculture expenses; (b) all stumpage fees will be directed
to MinFor's account to fund not only the incremental costs of this Project, but also to substitute for GoB
regular budget; and (c) MinFor can retain, to finance its own capital and operating costs (except for a
2 to 5% royalty payment to the national treasury), all the proceeds of its open market wood sales (under

the proposed liberalization of the wood markets and the introduction of the Wood Exchange). Table 7
below summarizes the budgetary impact of the proposed changes in the stumpage fees and the Project's
cost (those not funded by the IBRD loan) on MinFor cash flow, along with other expected cost increases
largely from fuel price adjusmens, etc. Without takg into account the results from open market sales,
the net result of the stunpage increases will be to reduce MinFor's budget dependency from 74% in 1993
to 0% by 1997, as seen in the last line of Table 7, "Net Budget (Deficit)/Surplus assuming phased
stumpage."
6.7         In addition, MiFor's proposed Wood Exchange will permit the capture of additional
revenues on wood sales from industrial processors and exporters. These additional profits could finance
both (a) future forestry capital investments; and (b) a national forestry contingency fimd (for fires, floods,
etc.), replacing GoB budgetary anmual and ad hoc allocations. The FSP will provide advisory services
on the financial restructuring of MinFor under this system of financing.

6.8           MnFor's absence of a capital base, and future severance from direct GoB budget funding
for ongoing operations,60 underscores the inportance of this Project's role in bridging funding through
its lending for operating costs. Loan disbursements will be instrumental in providing the bridge financing
for MinFor's working capital requirements during the 9-12 month period from the investment in
silviculture and harvesting equipment to the receipt of earnings from fuelwood and pulpwood sales. The
Bank's funds are MinFor's first infusion of borrowed money, which would establish MinFin's financial
framework for future corporatization of revenue earning activities. Following this transitional period,
MinFor will be able to sustain itself through revenmes generated from domestic and export sales of logs
and downstream sales products from MinFor's processing enterprises.
6.9           Wood Industries and Services. The Project will provide revenue earning opportunities
for both the Bellesprom and MinFor's enterprises in silviculture harvesting contracts and the processing
of smallwood into commercial products and will serve to encourage new enterprises in forest services
and wood processing. In addition there is are cost savings opporunities with the conversion of the
enterprises' heat-only-boilers to utilize fuelwood in place of imported fuel. To fully benefit from these
opportunities, small-scale investments would be required. The proposed EBRD Wood Processing Project
would provide this financing for selected Bellesprom enterprises.6' Financing for MinFor enterprises
could, in the future, be available from a proposed Bank loan for general industrial development (to be
on-lent through local financial intermediaries), provided that these enterprises have been privatized.
6.10          The Project will however, place downward pressure on both the cash flow and the
profitability of some of the Bellesprom and MinFor enterprises. The proposed increase in stumpage,
(and the parallel expected increase in fuel-related prices) and the proposed competition for harvesting
contracts and harvesting rights will force enterprises to become price competitive and efficient. It is
likely that some of tie weaker enterprises will close, while others will experience downsizing and
reductions in net profits. Those that make the necessary improvements in their operating efficiency will
form the backbone of the future wood processing industry.
Environmental Benefits
6.11          Intrinsic Value from Forestry. MinFor identifies the direct benefits to the economy
of a stand of forest in the following order of priority:
-      biodiversity protection and enacement
-      soil, water and fragile eco-systems conservation
-      carbon sequestration
-      national landscape, the forest heritage, recreation and tourism
-      commercial timber production
-      wildlife and other forest products production.
60.    Except as noted in footnote 59.
61.    The Bank's proposed Energy Sector Loan, now under preparation, is investigating this fuelwood utilization
and substitution opportunity in a broader industrial, municipal and electricity cogeneration context.



- 61 -
6.12          Improved natual resource management. New practices being introduced under this
Project will further promote these intrinsic benefits to the economy. Some examples are as follows:
(a)    Blodiversity and Wildlife.  Changes in silviculture practices will increase plant
biodiversity in the forests. This in tum will promote and support the wildlife.
(b)    Soil disturbance. Soil disturbance will be reduced during harvesting by the elimination
of skidding practices through the usage of loading mechanisms with trucks. Soil
impaction and erosion will also be reduced by the change in equipment.
(c)    Protection of sensitive eco-systems.  The proposed systematic monitoring of the
wetlands, and the changes in forestry operating regulations for these areas, will contribute
to the preservation of this ecosystem.
(d)    Oxygen. Increased carbon sequestration absorption from faster and healthier tree growth
is a benefit from the proposed intensive silviculture program.
6.13          Air pollution monitoring. Improved air pollution monitoring will assist to identify and
eliminate the source of pollution damage to the forests.
6.14          Radioactivity dispersal risk reduction. Reduction in the risk of the spread of radioactive
contamination from fires in the Chernobyl-contaminated forests is a direct benefit to public health in
Belarus and also in the international community.'
6.15          Health Benefits. The improvement in the systematic monitoring of forestry and industry
workers for radioactivity exposure will contribute to their health and the reduction in future health care
costs. Further the proposed systematic auditing of wood products for radioactivity contamination will
reduce the risks to public health, both domestically and internationally.
B. Economic Evaluation
6.16          The economic justification for the Project is driven by:
(a)    short-term benefits (the 1-5 year horizon) derived from the use of thinnings as a
substitute for imported fuel oil and coal in rural domestic and industrial heating, thus
saving foreign exchange, and from the introduction of more energy efficient machinery,
allowing for extraction of more timber per liter of imported gasoline used; and
62.    The radioactive cloud ftom the Chernobyl explosion is reported to have reahed as far west as Ireland and
as far north as Lapland. Radioactive particles re-suspended from the smoke plume of a major fire in the
Chernobyl forests could potentially reach just as far.



- 62 -
(b)    medium- and long-term benefits (the 20 - 100 year horizon) from higher yields of
existing forestry by increasing the size of commercially harvested trees, through both
intensive silviculture and thorough the introduction of improved species (e.g. the Seeds
Facility - para. 6.28).
Project ERR
6.17           The assumptions used in the economic analysis are discussed below, and the results are
summanzed in Table 8 below. The composite Economic Rate of Return (ERR) for the total project is
33%;e this composite average includes those Project components having quantifiable returns: namely
intensified silviculture, thinnings mechanization, spare parts, fire management, the seed facility and
benefit for the FMIS.
Intensified Slviculture
6.18         Assuming about one third of total smallwood production is consumed at the energy
equivalent oil import price, the combined return on the key commercial components of the Project,
silviculture with light mechanization of thinning, is 40%. This return is high because the Project
capitalizes on the sunk costs expended on the Belarus forests over the last 50 years. The incremental
benefits generated are significant due to: (i) the relatively light silviculture investment required relative
to the high benefits achieved through redress of years of neglected maintenance, and (ii) the cost
efficiencies and benefits saving achieved through light mechanization. However, this quantification of
the ERR methodology does not adequately capture the real benefits of the objective of this project - the
long-term improvement in the value of the standing wood (to be harvested in the 60th to 80th years after
the project). Therefore, the quantified ERR reflects the current value of fuelwood which is essentially
a by-product of the Project.
6.19         This component has been evaluated on the basis of five models of forest stands: one
representing the "without Project" case (no stand maintenance until age 40 years); and four "with Project'
cases (intensified silviculture applied to stands of ages 20, 25, 30, and 37 years) (Annex 10). These
models utilize MinFor yield tables adjusted for stand density under the assumed planting and silviculture
regimes to project wood production until maturity (100 years). Aside from quantifying the immediate
benefits of smallwood production from the thinning, the models differ in the size of trees remaining in
the stand, the numbers of trees that should be removed, and the volume of wood on an average tree
remaining. This reflects the long-run negative impact of neglect on stand productivity (the "without
Project" case), and the converse, the improvements in yield and production from early improved
maintenance (the "with Project" cases).
63.   This composite return is relatively higher than the overall returns of two forest projects presented to the
Board in 1993 - the Poland Forestry Development Support Project (Report No. 11318-POL) with an
overall ERR of 18%, and the Republic of Tunisia Second Forestry Development Project ( Report No.
11618-TUN) with an overall ERR of 13%. The Poland project is environmental and social-economically
onented. This explains the relatively higher unit investment costs (compared to the Belarus project) for the
volume of (short-term) thinnings production obtained. The Tunisia project's focus is largely environmental;
commercial benefits are a by-product of its implementation.

6.20         Costs of thinning and harvesting are based on the Project's light mechanization proposals.
International costs of this mechanization system (machinery and labor performance) were adjusted for
Belarus conditions, assuming reasonable levels of management. These models also assumed the
competitive advantages for efficiency and costs of contracted operations in the later years of the Project,
by comparison with less efficient force-account operations. Iternational prices (adjusted to the border)
for fuel were assumed. The economic value of incremental smallwood production is based in the energy
equivalent, i.e. the border price for oil at US$11.23 per cubic meter (Amex 9).



-64 -
6.21         The combined return on this component has been discussed in para. 6.18. To measure
the impact of the Project on the forest yield alone, the models assumed the same basic operating unit cost
structure for the "with Project" and "without Project" case. However, a truer measure of the Project's
economic impact is to reflect in the "without Project' case the inefficient operating costs of the heavy
USSR mechanization (para. 6.26).
6.22         The base case ERR for the intensive silviculture component, which considers a
conservative consumption level of 35% of total fuelwood and pulpwood produced, is 15%. The returns
depend on the assumptions made regarding rural domestic and industrial demand for fiuelwood and the
conversior. of heat-only fuel boilers to fuelwood utilization.
6.23         Sensitivity Test. The silviculture component is highly sensitive to the assumption that
the 20- to 40-year age class thinnings' smallwood harvest will be utilized as an energy substitute and sold,
and is insensitive to the major source of benefits - the long tern improved stand yield. From an ERR
perspective, a break even production level (i.e. ERR = 0%) is achieved when one third of fuelwood and
one third of pulpwood is sold at the oil substitute equivalent of US$11.23/m3.
6.24         Because the operational costs are largely variable, if market demand for fuelwood is not
realized, cash operational losses could be minimized, theoretically, by not engaging the labor and fuel
expenses to perform silviculture operations. Consequently, at minimum the financial losses would be the
invustment costs associated with the silviculture component. However, forgoing silviculture because of
under valued prices, or the absence of short-term demand for fuelwood, would be at the expense of
sacrificing the medium and long-term benefits of improved mature wood derived from proper and timely
thinning.
6.25         The high case scenario (Table 8) experiences accelerated returns because this sensitivity
exercise treats the operational costs as fixed under the assumption that the full silviculture program will
be executed for the long-tenn benefit of the forests and what is not consumed remains unsold, but
harvested, in the silviculture targeted areas.
LIt Mechanization
6.26         Returns to light mechanization are greatly dependent upon the efficiencies achieved
through reorganizing the work force around a changed technology (chainsaws and light mnchanization).
Under ideal conditions, an ERR of 126% is projected assuming 100% efficiency in the program. Under
a more realistic scenario, where a learning curve is assumed from the introduction of the new thnnings
methods and therefore where one third of the theoretical returns are assumed achievable in the first year
of the project, the ERR falls to 43 %.

Forest Fire Protection
6.29          Expenditures on improved fire protection would reduce by an estimated 30% the average
areas burned annually. The commercial value of a forest stand, assuming a 4% discount rate (a universal
standard hurdle rate for forestry investments) approximates US$1,256/ha; the environmental value is
considered to have at least an equivalent value. Given those assumptions, the investment in fire
protection generates an ERR of approximately 9%.
Forestry Management Information System
6.30         Introduction of a FMIS should provide savings to MnFor between $US 0.25 million and
$US 0.6 million; these savings are based on the 5% to 10% amnual operating cost savings expected from
FMIS utilization in comunercial forestry operations. The MinFor savings assume a conservative phased
savings schedule; from 2.5% to a maximum of 10% over the first four years after the PMIS is
introduced. Purther assumptions included: (a) software development will require substantial renewal after
10 years, and; (b) hardware would be replaced after five years. The ERR for the FMIS component is
22%.

C. Risks
6.31         The Project is subject to a range of external and internal risks as follows:
Risk external to the Project
(a)    Macro-economic reforms: the Project is based on the in-depth implementation of policy
refomis. Delays in implementation could result from political constraints (an election is
due soon in Belarus), or as deliberate GoB policy because structural adjustments to the
economy are viewed by policy makers as requiring a conservative pace of reform. This
will demand a similarly slower pacing of the sectoral reforms under this Project, and
therefore could cause a delay in the benefits from the sector policy reforms.
(b)    Sector policy reforms: a number of sector policy reforms change very significantly past
relationship and practices. There could be opposition form both the industry and local
governments. The project runs the risk of inadequate policy reform implementation
because potentially there may be reluctance to confront political pressures not to
implement certain sector policy reforms.
(c)    Exogenous Cost risks. Under proposed sector policy reforms, real price increases for
roundwood will be experienced by the wood processing industry at the same time that
fuel and fuel-related prices are expected to undergo a real price adjustment to reflect
international prices. The Project assumes that the wood industry will adjust its operations
to absorb much of these cost increases though significant improvements in operations
efficiencies and that they would not pass these costs on to the consumers. However these
adjustments require managerial skills and financing for new investments and industrial-
plant rehabilitation. Both these resources are scarce, and therefore the pace of the wood
industries' efficiency adjustments may be slower than the rate of cost increases. An
unequitable transition which passes most of the cost increases on to the consumer could
result in a further dampening of consumer demand for wood products, and therefore for
the demand for roundwood. It wold also reduce Belarus wood industry's international
price competitiveness.
In addition, as wood product prices are viewed by politicians as a significant component
in housing construction costs, political pressure may be brought to bear to again place
wood product prices under price control. This would rnm counter to the underlying
policy reforms on which this Project is built and re-introduce distortions in the wood
markets.
(d)    Demand risk. The Project assumes a reasonable level of sales of smallwood on the
domestic market as fuelwood and as industrial inputs. If the Belarus economic recovery
is at a slower rate than presently anticipated, domestic demand for wood products may
be dampened, and therefore wood sales will be reduced. This could result, in the
extreme, in an ERR of 7%  (para. 5.34), which remains acceptable for forestry
investments predicated primarily on medium- to long-term benefits. However if poor
sales reduce cash flows from wood sales and stumpage, this would also reduce the ability



- 67 -
of MinFin and MinFor to fumd the local cost component on the Project. The Project
scope would then need to be reduced.
The Project assumes a reasonable demand for wood product, and in particular fuelwood.
Any deterioration in short-term growth of the Belarus economy, and therefore a further
erosion of consumer spending power, will decrease the demand for wood products,
dampening open market based opportunities and operations on which the effectiveness of
the proposed sectoral reforms depends.
(e)    Market risk. The Project assumes the effective operation of an open market in wood
in place of the state product allocation system. For this to be effective, present wood and
wood product purchasers would need to obtain working capital to replace the state
allocation rights and to enable them to purchase wood on the proposed open market.
Given the current state of the local financial system, this may prove to be a significant
constraint in the development and effective fiuctioning of the wood industries and
therefore the wood markets. The risks are: (i) an artificially dampening market demand
for wood, and; (ii) that the Wood Exchange will not be successful during its initial
period, and will discourage its initiators from pursuing its effective functioning over the
longer term.
Risks Internal to the Project
(t)   Inadequate Local Financing. GoB is operating under a very tight budget ceiling to
comply with IMF conditionality, and therefore is unwilling to increase budgetary
allocations to MinFor to cover the local costs of this Project. GoB's therefore plans to
finance its contribution to the Project's local costs (US$11.6 million) from real increases
in the stumpage fee. However, if market demand for wood products is lower than
plamned, and if the wood industries face cash constraints because of the inadquac of
their working capital resource, either they will delay payment of the stumpage fees by
increasing their indebtedness to MinFin, or refuse to accept the real increases in the fees
on the grounds that the financial burden places the enterprises at risk. Given the political
sensiuvity of lost industrial workplaces, and therefore the stumpage fee, both scenarios
are realistic, and could result in only insignificant real increases in stumpage revenues
to MinFin. The consequences would be inadequate cash resources to finance the GoB
share of local costs of the Project. The only mitigating actvity could be significant
efforts by BeUlesprom, in conjunction with GKI, to restructure and refinance the major
wood enterprises and to plan for efficiency improvements. However this is not yet
forthcoming, in spite of the fact that the privatization of the BefUesprom enterprises is
high on GoB's priority list. Any decline in the sales of fuelwood (at prices without
GoB's consumer subsidy for fuelwood) will have a similar negative impact on MinFor's
cash flows.
(g)   Implementation Risk. This is the Bank's first investment Project in Belarus. While the
Project is within the management capabilities of the senior staff of MinFor, the
complexities of compliance with Bank procedures for procrement, accounting,
disbursements, etc., will not be easily accomplished. However the proposed staff
training programs to be conducted by the PIU consultants should mitigate these risks.



- 68 -
There remains however a risk of implementation delays in the procurement of consulting
services and equipment. Given the plan to provide a nine month lead period for pre-
implementation preparation and planning ahead of the 1995 silviculture season, this risk
should be manageable.
(h)    MuFor Enterprise Incentives. Initial intensified silviculture operations will be carried
out under MinFor's force account. The implementation plan is ambitious, at about a 33%
increment over MinFor's rayon-level enterprises present annual silviculture maintenance
areas. The appraisal mission and the Project Director-designate have assessed that both
the managerial and the manpower capacity is available at the local level to implement
this program. The mitigating factor provided by the Project would be training at the
local level prior to Project implementation. However, the enabling enviromnent may not
have cianged sufficiently, due to inadequate macro- and sector-policy reforms, to deliver
the incentives for sound management practices.
(i)    Private Sector Development. The Project plans to provide competitive access to the
private sector to harvest contracts that in the past were automatcally assigned to either
MhFor-enterprises or to Bellesprom. If however the private sector contractors do not
emerge during this period of transition, the Project objective of achieving operating
efficiencies through promoting a competitive private sector in forestry services may not
be achieved.



- 69 -
PART VII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
7.1          Loan Covenants and Schedules: During Negotiations the following agreements were
included as covenants to the Loan Agreement, or in appropriate Schedules to the Loan Agreement:
(a)   that by December 31, 1994, GoB would issue relevant decrees and regulations to firther
facilitate the liberalization of international trade in wood end wood products in a manner
agreed between GoB and the Bank (para. 3.12)
(b)   that by December 31, 1994, relevant decrees and regulations, agreed between GoB and
the Bank, would be issued by GoB for the further liberalization of the domestic wood
market in wood and wood products, except for procurement of such products required
under the State Order system (para. 3.15);
(c)   that the "wood market facility" would be established by GoB, before the end of 1994,
under Articles of Association, regulations and operating policies agreed between GoB and
the Bank (para. 3.16);
(d)   on the establishment, by October 31, 1994, of a stumpage and royalty policy agreed
between GoB and the Bank, for implementation beginning January 1, 1995 (para. 3.19);
(e)   that procurement procedures for all goods, civil works and consultant services would be
applied in accordance with Bank guidelines and that progress reports would include
monitoring of the various aggregate amounts and ceilings (para. 3.56);
(f)    on the terms and conditions for the operations of the Special Account (para. 3.62);
(g)   on the establishment of separate Project accounts and the appointment of exteral auditors
(para. 4.35); and
(h)   that the ammal audit of the Project's accounts would be competed and the final annual
audit report delivered by the external auditors, through the PIU to the Bank, no later than
the end of March each year, for the previous year's accounts (para. 4.37).

reform measures, in coordination and in a manner acceptable to the Bank (para. 3.11 );

at market prices (para. 3.15);
(d)   that for the establishment of the formal wood -market facility," GoB would find an
appropnate legal structure for this market under which GoB's exchange taxes would not
be applicable (para. 3.17);
(e)   that the new Forest Law, presently before the Parliament, establishes the State's sole
right to the nation's wood resources and that, after passage of this law, GoB will
promlgate enabling regulations to establish (i) MinFor as its agent for the market
disposition of the State's wood resources under MinFor's management, and (ii) the
State's full rights of use of all funds so generated (para. 3.18);
(f)    that the Bank considered a royalty fee of between 2 and 5% of gross annual MinFor
wood sale revenues to be a reasonable level; that the royalty fees should be payable to
the national treasury only at the point of the first transfer of ownership from MinFor;
and that for those wood products on which a royalty fee is charged, no stumpage is
payable and visa versa (para. 3.19);
(g) that certain anmnual MinFor costsm were not expected to be recovered from the stumpage
fee, and would continue to be funded from the national budget; and that the target
theoretical total stumpage revenue would be that amount required to fully recover the
annual operating costs of MinFor's commercial silviculture operations, if recovered from
the theoretical annual total harvest (para. 3.20);

million for 15 years, including a grace period of 5 years, at the Bank's standard variable interest rate.

ANNEX 2

BELARUS
Forestry Development Project
Annex 2: Forestry Sector Policy Reform - hnplementation Timetable
Introduction
1.    The agreements on economic policy reforms reached by the Government of Belams and the
International Monetary fund (under the ST) and the World Bank (under the Rehabilitation Loan) provides
the basis for policy reform implementation at the sectoral level. There is no longer a question of "if" or
"what reforms", but now the focus shifts to "how and when." This paper presents the initial proposals
for the implementation timetable for the minima reforms for the forestry sector. The specific sector
policy reforms were presented to the Government in June 1993 in the form of a policy matrix
(Attachment 1).
2.    It should be noted that in addition to the overall policy reform agreement, the Government has
place the wood industries high on the list of priority sectors to be privatized.
Policy Reforms
3.     The six areas of reform proposed as high priority for the forestry sector are as follows:
*     Increases in the Stunpage Fees
*     Increased comptitiveness in harvesting and the creation of private sector
harvesting companies
*     increasing competition in the marketing of standing timber and harvested logs
*     removal of price and profit controls on wood and wood products
*     removal of all export controls for timber, and removal of export taxes on timber
and wood products, excet on those items (if any) where administerd prices will
remain in force
*     removal of product allocation and state order system.
All six of these sector-specific reforms are inter-related and need to be implemented under an integrated,
phased and complementary schedule.
Timig
4.    The overall time-frame for the implementation of these reforms is 1993 to 1996. This
corresponds to the start-up of the proposed Forestry Development Project, and includes in 1993 the
announcements to the public and in particular to the wood industry the full extent and the implications
of all the proposed reforms. There are three subgroups of reforms based on the timing as shown on the
table following:

Detailed Implementaion Program
Subgroup I - Ongoing:.
Stume Fees Adjustment
5.    The objective of this reform is to remove the subsidy element to both the wood industry and to
the Ministry of Forestry that has resulted for the pricing of standing wood far below its economic and
commercial value.
6.    To increase immediately the stumpage fees tot he full commercial value of the standing timber
would created too much of a price shock to the wood processing industry. The industry needs time to
adjust its operations and to increase its operating and utilization efficiency to adequately absorb the costs
increases without fWlly passing these costs in to the consumers.
7.    The wood industries will need to invest in equipment to improve efficiency, both to counter the
increased in fuel and electricity costs (through the introduction of energy conservation investment) and
the increased wood costs (better utilization, less waste, better quality control, changed processes, better
waste utilization, etc.). At the same time management sldlls and labor-efficiency will have to be
improved. There are savings to be made in the reorganization of the wood yards, the correct selection
of equipment, the better management of the labor force, improved safety and work conditions, etc.,
improved product design, introduction of new products etc.
8.    It should be noted that the privatization of the wood industries, the introduction of competition



ANNEX 2
page 3 of 14
in the wood markets and in harvesting will be complementary actions to ensure the competitiveness of
the wood industries and there cost effectiveness.
9.    It is therefore proposed that the target stumpage fees to be announced in late 1993 together with
the explanation of the future stumpage policy. This target fee - the full economic value of standing wood
-- should be achieved through the gradual real price increases over the next three (broadleaf species) to
five years (conifers). In the interim it is proposed that the stumpage fee be increased in both nominal
and real terms once every three months (to allow for inflation compensation), by preannounced real
increase steps. The actual increase will also need to be adjusted for inflation.
10.   In addition it is proposed that a stumpage fee be introduced for immature woods removed during
thinnings, and that this fee be based in the commercial value by age class or size. This will become
increasingly important as smallwood attains a commercial uses and value through the introduction of
processing facilities and through the development of the export markets for pulpwood.
11.   The introduction of quarterly adjusted stumpage fees will also require a change in administration
of the assessment and collection of the stumpage fees. Presendy stands are allocated on an anmnal basis
to harvesters/processors, mostly Bellesprom enterprises. Bellesprom and other harvesters/processors pay
the stumpage fee at some undefined time after harvest. This has resulted in a very significant reduction
in the value of the stumpage fees collected by the national treasury as a result of inflation erosion.
12.   From January 1994 it is proposed that:
a)    harvested blocks continue to be allocated on an anmual basis;
b)    the harvester, at thk time of allocation, provides MinFor with a quarterly harvest plan;
c)    the harvester pays the stumpage fees, at the stumpage rate for that quarter, directly to the
stumnmae account at the National Bank at the end of each quarter, in accordance with that
quarter's harvest plan,and the amounts in the BELGOSLES inventory for that stand, and
IRRESPECTIVE of actual harvest;'
d)    non-payment of quarterly stumpage fees on time automatically results in cancellation of
harvest rights on all allocated blocks, and there return to MinFor for disposal to another
harvester/processor;
e)    MinFor is responsible for reporting on expected and actual stumpage fees to the national
treasury, also on a quarterly basis;
f)    non-completion of the annual plan by a harvester will result in the either cancellation of
the stand allocation, or reduced allocation for the next year. As the stumpage fee for the
non-harvested stand would already have been paid, this would be forfeited as a fine for
non-performance, or if the stand is harvested within the first quarter of the next year, not
forfeited.
13.   This proposed plan will require an increased and more active MinFor inspection function of
harvesting activities, and will demand an more accurate BELGOSLES stand inventory to ensure the
minimizaion of discrepancies between actual volumes and quality of timber and those stated in the
including the case where the harvester has not harvested that stand, or has not completed the
harvesting. Payment of stumpage, therefore, would no longer be inked to the actual harvest activity, but
becomes a payment for the right to harvest.



ANNEX 2
page 4 of 14
inventory. The quality target should be no more than a 5% discrepancy, while today it is reported that
the inventory inaccuracy is as much as 20%. This is especially serious of the high value timber stands,
even though they make up only a small part of the amual harvest.
14.   Related golia: all the increased revenues from stunpage should be allocated to the annual
operating and investment budget of the Ministry of Forestry. It is the itention that withi the five year
period that the Ministry of Forestry activities should be full funded from the annal stumpage revenues
and revenues from sales of wood and wood products. There could be a surplus from these revenues that
could be used for:
a)    the creation of national fund for forest fire and other natural disasters (e.g flooding);
b)    contributions of forestry R&D and forestry education; and
c)    general contributions to the state and local budgets.
Subgroup I - mmedlate:
15. The three reforms in this sub-group should be announced to the public and the wood industry as
early as possible in the last quarter of 1993 so that the enterprises in particular can make their plans and
necessary adjustments to be ready for 1994.
A:    Price and profit controls
16. There is little justification for considering wood and wood products to be strategic items requiring
price or profit controls. While the policy was already in existence in 1993 that there should be no price
controls on these products, in fact the controls remained, often imposed at the regional and district level.
17.   It is proposed therefore that before the end of 1993 a specific decree be issued covering all wood
products that removed all these controls, and prevents the local authorities for imposing such controls.
18.   However, there remains the social issue of the price for fuel wood. This needs to be dealt with
within the context of the social safety net for vulnerable groups, and the financing (subsidies) provided
to the Ministry of Forestry's local enterprises for the subsidized sale of firewood ONLY to those eligible
under the social safety net program. The details of the management of the fuelwood component should
be included in the proposed decree.
19.   In the explanatory efforts that have been suggested that the Government undrtakes to explain the
new sectoral policies, it should be stated that there is an expected price increase in wood products due
to the increases in fuel and standing wood prices. However the goverriient should also state that it
expects the wood processing companies to improve there efficiency and not to pass all the price increases
onto the consumers.
B:    Allocations aO.SaeOders
20.   As of Jamnary 1 1994 all wood processing enterprises should be free to produce what ever product
they choose and to sell to whomever they choose (see later on export liberalization). All these state
enterprises that had expected to receive allocation of wood and wood products should therefore be given
the tie in the latter part of 1993 to reorganize themselves to enter the wood market as active purcss



page 5 of 14
as of the beginning of the new year. Similarly all the wood enterprises should now begin to organize
their marketing/sale departments, as they will no longer have 'captive customers."
C:    Timber and Wood Products Export and Import Liberalization
21.   All restrictions on roundwood, cut timber and wood products exports and imports should be lifted
as of January 1, 1994. Licenses should not be required. The Government therefore needs during the
next three months to review all docmentaion requement hat pre-suppose the existence of an export
license, and to remove these requirements. For wood products a reduction in the export tariffs should
be immediately put in place, in line with the general agreement on tariff reforms.
22.   It is to be anticipated that there will be criticism that Belarus should not be exporting its scarce
wood resourmces. However the findameals of the econonic reform is based on open market trading.
The removal of price controls and the product allocation system will simultaneously permit the market
to adjust supplies to satis  market demands. There wil in general, after a short period of market
adjustment, be no shortage of wood or wood products in the local markets. Prices will be higher than
today, but that will reflect the adjustment to the real economic value of wood, and the opportunities in
neighboring markets.
23.   Liberalized wood imports, and estabishng a neutral trade regime that does not protect the local
wooaindustry from competition from imports-all to be in place by the beginning of 1994, wi be
e-sential tO ensure that the market remains adequately supplied with product and does not suffer from
artificial shortages.
Sub-oup m  - Phased:
24. Both items in this sub-group require the development of entrepreneurs and market structures.
They also require testing of systems of management by the Ministry of Forestry and other wood
enterprises, therefore it is recommended that these reforms be phased in three steps: Pilot; Regional; and
National at yearly intervals.
25.   Both reforms are closely related: the introduction of contract harvesting on behalf of the Ministry
of Forestry implies sale of assortments at roadside. Therefore the mechanism of the marketing of
assortnents has to be developed, together with the assurance that there will be competition at these
markets. The development of the privatization of Bellesprom enterprises also plays a crucial role in the
development of the competitive market place. So does the development of new wood industries
(hopefully to be financed under the EBRD project).
A:    Contract Harvesting Development
26. For the beginning of January 1995, the Ministry of Forestry should select at least three rayon
in the three non-Chernobyl oblasts. In these three rayon that would for 1995 n_o allocate standing wood
blocks to th 'ocal Bellesprom entrpris. Instead they would announce a harvesting program of stands,
including the details of the wood inventories of those stands, and call for harvesting contractors to provide
MinFor with bids on the cost of harvestiDng of selected stands.



ANNEX 2
page 6 of 14
27.   The rayons chosen should preferably be those where there is more than one wood processing
industry operating (Bellesprom, MinFor and other private enterprises), where there are a good number
of wood enterprises in the neighboring rayons. Also for the selected rayons there should be established
harvesting companies or groups (i.e. parts of a Bellesprom or MinFor complex), or a suitable number
of such companies in neighboring rayon's all of whom would wish to place bids. The companies'
harvesting capacity should be larger than the wood volume to be harvested.
28.   MinFor would manage the contract harvesting, if necessary leasing the contractor machinery from
MinFor's machinery pool. The Contractors would need to be pre-qualified, and prove that they have had
training in harvesting and understand the silvicultural conditions and standards to be met, including all
the environmental safeguards, post-harvest site preparation. etc.
29.   To be pre-qualify to be a harvest contractor, the site managers of the harvesting company would
have had to attend a harvesting course, and to have passed the qualifying examinations. This course
would be provided by MinFor, possible through Kirov institute or with foreign experts, in mid-to-late
1994, fimded under the World Bank project.
30.   MinFor would also train its enterprise staff in harvesting management, and also in harvesting
inspection. MinFor would increase its activities in on-site inspection of harvesting, while the harvesting
is in progress, as well as the inspection upon completion. MinFor would also provide the supervision
staff for the management of the road-side assortment site, but the assortment and selection would be the
responsibility of the harvesting contractor.2
31.   For 1996, with adjustments to the procedures, two of the three oblasts where pilots were held
would in their totality come under this system. That means that no standing wood allocations would be
made to Bellesprom or any other concern in the oblasts in that year, or in the future.
32.   Training for potential harvesting contractors wold be held in these oblasts in mid to late 1995,
to ensure an adequate number of pre-qualified contractors. Arrangements would be put into place for
leasing of equipment if this is necessary.
33.   In a similar way in 1997 the system would be applied to the entire country, with training courses
in the latter part of 1996.
34.   Consequendy at the beginning of 1997 no firther allocations of standing wood would be made
to any concern or enterprise for their own harvesting and processing. MinFor would manage all clear
felling at all sites, through the operations of harvesting contractors.
35.   Under this system of contracted harvesting, MinFor would also achieve the harvesting of difficult
sites. But for these it would have to negotiate a harvest cost with the contractors, as it wold be unlikely
that there would be a willingness to bid if easier sites were also on offer. In addition, for difficult and
especially remote site, it may be necessary to occur additional costs of terrain or road transport of the
assortments to a more convenient road-side market site. MinFor would have to recover the higher cost
2  MinFor would manage roadside sales and the supervision of ttansport conaors



ANNX
page 7 of 14
of harvesting of these sites through the correct site-class adjustment of the stunpage fee.
B:    Establishing Comnpetitive Roundwood Markets
36.   Those enterprises that normally would have had wood allocated to them either under the allocation
system, or through the allocation of standing wood to Bellesprom, would now have the opportunity to
buy roundwood at roadside in a regular basis.
37.   MinFor can choose to sell at roadside at announced prices (the minimum would be stumwage +
harvesting cost), or for the high value tinber, at auction. at the very least pilot auctions for hardwoods
should be started in 1995.
38.   Under this system of road-side sales MinFor would be responsible to pay the stumpage to the
Treasury.
39.   MinFor can add to these roadside markets smallwood, pulpwood and fuelwood from its own
thinnings of the forests. It shoild be noted that, with the liberalization of exports, these markets would
also be a source of supply for wood exporters.
40.   In parallel to the establishment of the roadside markets MinFor would inform all wood buyers
as early as mid 1994 that it would be their responsibility to transport wood from the road side from 1995
onwards in the pilot areas, and from 1996 onwards in the selection oblasts, and 1997 nation-wide. This
should encourage the emergence of new wood transport enterprises.
