BELARUS
Forestry Development Project
Annex 3: Potential Harvesting Levels in Belarus
Historical Background
1.           Up to and including the period of the Second World War, the Byelorussian forests were
severly depleted by overcutting and destruction. Since then there has been a serious effort to restore the
forest resource which today cover 34% of the national territory.
2.           The last ten years, annually harvested volumes have been kept safely within the sustained
yield principles by following carefully elaborated management plans for each forest. Each management
plan is based on a forest inventory which is repeated every 10 years. When planning the anmnal cut the
individual management plans are summed up to national level in order to arrive at the actual allowable
cut (AAC). In average over the last six years the actual cut has been 5% lower than the allowable cut
for the final fellings. The amount of thinnings is more dependant on the funds available from the state
budget and on the markets for small wood. Because of a very small proportion of mature wood, there
has been periods in the past where the clear cut volume has been above the allowable cut, but no serious
differences have occured since the 1960's.
3.           Reliable statistics on the actual cut exists only for the forests managed by the Ministry
of Forestry. The development of those cuts the last six years is illustrated in Figure 1. The harvest on
other torests is approximately 0.8 million m3 ob.
4.           It is notable how the cut has been steadily declining during this period in spite of an age
distribution which would indicate a possible increase in hanresting levels (Figure 2). The major causes
of this decline has been the reduction of areas due to radioactive contamination and the reduced demand
for forest products in Europe and the FSU states.
5.           The volumes presented in Figure 1 represent the total harvest, including fuelwood, which
is normally waste utilised by the local population. The industrial wood which actually reaches the
processing industry is only about 60% of the harvested volume. This low utilisation is most likely a
reflection of the fact that there is a good denand for local fuelwood, while industrial utilisation of small
wood has been limited to a few board mills and export to Scandinavia.
Poteni  Hrvest
6.           The International Institute for Applied Systems Analysis (IIASA) published in 1992
forecasts of potential harvest for 0The Forest Resource of the Former European USSR.' This study is
a major effort to analyse the vast resources where Belarus is a small but important part. Detailed results
of the study are not available at Byelorussian level but a summary indicate a harvesting level of 15.9
million m3 ob as a long term average. This level is compared with the actual harvest which according
to IIASA was 12.3 million mr3 ob in 1988. As this figure in fact is 10.9 milion m3 ob (10.1 for Ministry
of Forestry and 0.8 for other owners) according to the Ministry of Forestry in Belarus, it is likely that
IHASA has used a figure that was planned for 1988.

7.           It is concluded in the IHASA study that the actual cut is well within the forecasted values
but in several cases higher than the presented AAC. The reason for this is that the AAC is only calcu-
W   for the final dear cut, while in Belarus a considerable part of the cut comes from the thinnings.
In order to estimate the total AAC (including the thinnings), the Soviet experts assume a factor of 1.12
which for Byelorussian conditions, with only 2.4% of the forest in mature age, must be considered a
serious underestimation.
8.           In order to form an independant view on above figures, the appraisal mission carried out
simple simulations of the biological potential of the Byelorussian forests. The basis for these calculations
were as follows:
*     The forest areas managed by the Ministry of Forestry under protection and conservation
objectives were reduced in order to reflect the restrictions on the thinning and clear cut
levels in such forests. The remaining areas by species and age were used as a basis for
the calculations.
*     Models for harvesting schedules on a per hectare basis were constructed using the basic
volume produrtion from the Byelorussian yield tables for six different species (pine,
spruce, oak, birch, aspen and alder). The yield class used for each model was selected
in order to compnsate for growth losses due to defoliation and the fact that the forest
inventory reports two thirds of the Byelorussian forests as not being fully closed.



ANNheX 3
page 3 of 6
*     The barvestmg schedules included estimated assortment distribution and harvesting costs
for each cut.
O     Three scenarios were calculated:
(i)    Mininum case: all areas with radioactive contaniination were excluded;
(ii)   Middle case: all areas with contamination levels exceeding 5 Ci/lc2 weic
excluded;
(iii) MNximum case: all areas with contamination le-vels exceeding 5 Ci/lan2 were
excluded, but areas of mature and maturing forest with a contamintion ievel af
1-15 Ci/km2 were assumed to be clear cut during a three year period in order to
salvage some of the wood before contamination levels increase further.
9.           The results of the simulations are shown in Figures 3 to 5 following. It is important to
stress that the estimated volumes are theoretical potentials and do not pretend to reflect an r-tual long
term harvesting plan. In reality the large variations in cutting levels will be smoothed out by adjustments
of rotation ages and eventual exparsion of the forest asset.
10.          These cases as based on the the age and species distribution of the Byelorussian forests,
as well as the contamination levels for different age classes (Figure 2). It is interestir, to note that the
proportion of contaminated forests (percentage above the bars representing contaminated areas in Figure
2) is highest for ihe youngest forests.

Conclusions
11.          Although there is ahistory of overcutting inBelams and in the CIS, there is no indication
that present cutting levels are surpassing the sustained level. Some litterature (notably "Regional
Alternaives in Soviet limber Management" by BreLton Barr) pointing to an exhaustion of the commercial
forests in Belarus, Ukraine and the Baltics, evidenly refer to the fact that very little mature forest exist
in those areas, and that it is being rapidly diminished by clear cuts. However, those figures are based
on a forest inventory published in 1983, while later figures for Belarus indicate that the proportion of
mature forest is increasing to close to 5% after having been 2.4% in 1988. It is also debatable what is
the age limit for manre forest. Historcal figures are based on rotations which were set in order to
maximise production of high quality logs. By introducing economic considerations when deciding on
rotation length it is likely that the rotations will come down slighdy. Efforts to bridge over the foreseen
supply explosion in the 2010's are also likely to reduce rotations during a period preceeding 2020.
12.          There are strong indications that considerable larger volumes could be harvested than what
is done at present. The increase should be principally in thinning volumes. Both the forecast produced
by UIASA and the appraisal mission are rough estimates and would have to be made with more
sophistication in order to be a reliable estimate of sutained yield harvesting volumes in Belarus.



ANNEX 3
page 6 of 6
13.          It is therefore recommended that more detailed simulations are carried out under the
framework of the Porestry Strategic Plan. Such .sinulations should specificatly adress the following
issues:
*     How large conservation and protection areas are required in Belams? In what way do
these areas influence the sustained harvesting levels?
*     How large areas should be set aside of the conminated areas? When could harvesting
be reintroduced in these areas? What is the safest management scheme for these forests?
Q     How could the violent changes in wood -* ply over time, depending on the skewed age
distribution, be smoothed irnto a long temi sustained level? What are the forest
maaement consequences of such a stratv? Is it desirable?

BELARUS
Forestry Development Project
Annex 5: Operations of the Ministry of Forestry
Introduction
1.    The Ministry of Forestry (MinFor) comprises 86 enterprises, most of which are vertically
integrated operations entailing silviculture, harvesting, and wood processing/sawing activities. The
enterprises vary in size and production levels and some have comparative advantages over others due to
equipment, access to the forest resource and size and quality of the resource. Since structured under a
socialistic regime, all enterprises have operated with the objective of producing targeted production levels
of wood products at national unit cost guidelines estabLished by Goseconplan and MinFor.
2.    Under the 1992 and 1993 planning guidelines of MinFin and Goseconplan, MinFor supervised
the harvest of approximately 5.3 million cubic meters of mature wood annually. Of this total harvest,
approximately 70 percent or 3.8 million cubic meters was allocated to Bellesprom for its own harvest and
downstream activities with the remaining 1.3 million cubic meters was allocated to the enterprises under
MinFor. Additionally, MinFor supervised silviculture operations engaged on approximately 269,000 ha
ainually, which yielded approximately 3.3 million cubic meters of thinnings. In 1992, 1.3 million cubic
meters of the 3.3 million cubic total was sold as industrial wood; about half of this was sold within the
MinFor enterprises tbemselves for downstream wood processing use and the other half sold outside of
the MinFor enterprises. The balance of approximately 1.9 million cubic meters was sold as fuelwood,
mostly to war veterans and the elderly at highly subsidized prices.
3.    In 1992, revenue from the sale of thinnings totaled 479 million rubles or US$ 479,000 (assuming
an exchange rate of 1,000 rubles = US$1); this represents a profit contribution from MinFor's
silviculture actvities and together with slddding activities, contributed 15 percent to MinFor's budget.
Of the total thinning sales, industrial wood generated approximad US$379,000 in revenue and firewood
US$ 100,000, or US$ 0.29 and US$ 0.05 per cubic meter, respectively.
Accounting
4.    Both the future administrative level of MinFor as well as the forest enterprises require a financial
and accounting system modeled after standard intenational accounting practices and operating in tandem
with a Forestry Management Information System (FMIS). Additionally the system should promote both
budget management and long-range planning activities. At the MinFor administration level, an
acountig system is required to integrate a forestry management system with a cost accountng system,
capturing all actual costs by forest actfvity and properly measuring the financial performance as a profit
center. Additionally, a cash management system woa" provide a procedure to distribute revenue from
wood sales or contactual services through the proposed Wood Exchange to each individual enterprise.
Both the cost and cash management systems would be integrated into a managerial accounting system
tailored to MinFor's activities. Additionally, an overhaul of MinFor's financial accounting system is
required to: 1) reflect Western accountig procedures, 2) accommodate a proper external audit by
cerdfied acountants, and 3) to accommodate the revised activities of MinFor.



ANNEX S
page 2 of 5
5.    At the rayon level, individual enterprise also require a similar overhaul of a managerial and
financial accounting system to ammodate their future commercialized role. The overal accounting
should be tailored to each enterprise's niix of silviculture, harvesting, wood processing and transportation
activities.
The Wood Exchange
6.    Outlined below are the type of service or wood products to be traded on the proposed Wood
Exchange as well as the owners of the products/service concerned prior to exchange sale:
Service/Product                                Seller
Service:
thinninga                                    MinFin
standing stock for final fellings b          MinFin
Product:
logged wood                                  MinFin, enterprise
semi-manufactured products (chips)           manufacture, trader
products from minor forest use               enterprise
7.    There are several fund flows into the exchange which could be used to sustain either the
administrative costs of the exchange alone or all the administrative costs of MinFor, including payment
for contracted silviculture (thinning) operations. These funds flows include revenue received through the
exchange from the sale of thinming, logged wood, and stumpage received from auctioned harvesting
operations. Alternatively, a transaction fee could be charged for the sale of wood products on the
exchange with the proceeds flowing to finance part or all of the activities of MinFor depending upon
MinFin's future intentions for subvention.
Stumpage
8.    A number of different options MinFin might consider for funding of the Forestry Development
Project would include the introduction of the wood market (as now proposed by the Ministry of Forestry)
and a real increase in the stumpage. A phased real price adjustments to stumpage fees to fully reflect the
total operating costs of MinFor's activities, and including a reserve for the long term investments required
to maintain the economic value of the Byelorussian forests.
thinning are service activities to be contracted out to the enterprises but supervised and
regulated by MinFor; the roundwood yield from thinning activity would belong to the Treasury and
could be sold on the Exchange to either an end user for fuelwood, to an enterprise as a raw material
source for semi-mamufactured products or directly overseas to a perspective buyer.
b Standing mature stock can either be sold to a private party or contracted out for harvest. The
end products from both courses of action would ultimately be channeled to the exchange. Under the
contract harvest arrangement, the Treasury remains the owner of the felled timber.



page 3 of S
9.    The benefits of this adjustment to stunmpage are:
1)    increased stumpage would relieve the financial burden imposed on MFin in subsidizig
the annual operations of MinFor;
2)    increased stumpage would promote the financial self-sufficiency of MinFor by funig
its operations directly through the sale of end products priced at market values.
3)    severing MinFor's financial link to the national budget will require MinFor to implement
efficiency measures in reducing its own budget to compete with roundlog madres
existing outside of Belarus.
10.   There are disadvantages of the increased stumpage fees. There will be hier raw material costs
to the downstream wood processing sector which would are presumed to be passed on to the consumer.
Yet these wood price increases are expected to be lower than the expected real incrases in fuel prices.
There are efficiencies that the wood processing industries can adopt to adjust to these charges in ral input
costs without passing all the costs onto the consumers. But for these adjustments to be achieved, a
competitive wood product market, and liberalized international trade in wood products will be necessy.
11.   The table attached presents the on-going budget and incremental budget for MinFor's silviculture
activities alone; all other activities including wood processing are consider to be self-financing and require
no treasury subvention. The derived "Stumpage required for a zero deficit" (line 18) would cover the
total costs of MinFor's existing budget assuming no further subvention from MinFi. This is based on
MinFor's base budget (line 8) adjusted for the incremental costs from anticipated price incrases in fuel
(ine 12), expanded silviculture activides under this project and thereafter (line 10) and other necessazy
increases in ancillary activities (e.g. fire protection, seeds, regulatory activities, etc. - line 14)
Alternatively, a phased increase in the stumpage fee (line 19) is possible which decreases the annud
subsidy element to zero over a four year time frame (line 20).
12.   As an alternative to a direct increase in the stumpage fee for clear cut wood alone, stumpage fees
could be charged on the thinning wood sold to Bellesprom to reflect its true cost of production. A
stumpage charge on thinnings would permit a lower stumpage charge for mature wood. Open market
sales of all wood by MinFor would eliminate the need to set a stumpage fee, and MinFor could finance
its activities from the proceeds of these open market sales. MinFor has proposed such a market.

Annex 9: Economic Analysis
A. Economic Price Assumptions
1.           The price of the products concerned is the most critical factor to the success of the
project. Table 1 below is a summary of the prices used in the various scenarios discussed in section B,
Component Analysis.
Table 1: Price Assumptions
Economic Wood Product: Prices: (US$/m)                Reference:
Fuelwood (oil equivalent import parity)   $11.23   Table 9.1
Sawlogs (export parity)   $64.43   Table 9.2
Veneerlogs (export parity)  $130.86   Table 9.3
Standard Conversion Factors (SCF):         _
Labor     0.87l
Foreign exchange     1.00
Fuel     1.00 
2.           The economic value of the fuelwood and pulpwood over the project life will be largely
a function of new wood consumption patterns, influenced by (a) rising oil prices, growth in personal
disposable incomes with concomitant expenditure on consumer durables - e.g. furniture - and investment
in housing, etc.; (b) investments in new wood processing facilities; and, (c) to a lesser degree, regional
economic activity and inter-regional distribution patterns.
3.           The consumption patterns for fuelwood and pulpwood are expected to change from
industrial use to predominantly fuel use, if imports of energy from Russia are no longer subsidized and
prices rise sharply to international prices. This is anticipated during 1994. Even if under the planned
customs union with Russia, fuel prices do not rise to their full international value, there will still be a
significant real price increase in fuel. In addition, recent measures to remove subsidies and cross
subsidies for all types of fuels will increase the costs of fuel to both industrial and domestic consumers.
Fuelwood is expected to substitute for oil in both rural domestic and ural industrial heating and
consequently can be valued at the import parity price of fuel oil, equivalent to $11.23/me (Table 9.1).



-jMX9
page 2 of 22
The costs of adapting oil-fueled boilers for fuelwood has also been incorporated into the analysis of this
import parity price.
B. Project Component Analysis
4.           Disussed below '. the main assumptions behind those project components (intensified
silviculture, harvesting, spare parts, fire management, and the seed facility) which have quantifiable,
derived economic values:
Intenuifid Silvicuture
5.           This component of the project would aim at reducing the country's backlog in immature
stand maintenace, with a thinning program to cover an area of 280,000 ha over three years which will
increase small wood production in the short run and more valuable large-size timber in the long run.
Two benefit/cost streams are associated with intensified silviculture: one stream represents the incremental
smallwood production and increased value of the remaining standing wood, as a result of thiuning
280,000 ha targeted area, using a new intensified silviculture practice; and the second stream measures
the thimin operations costs savings derived from the introduction of light mechanization to replace the
present heavy ex-USSR equipment. This second stream of benefits is analyzed separately under "Light
Mechanization" (parn. 12 et seq. below).
6.    Incremental benefits from the harvested and standing wood values alone, i.e. the first stream,
have been measured by contrasting a modelled "afkr" thinnings program with on-going MinFor thinming
activities (Annex 10, Tables 10.1-10.5). The targeted areas have not been adequately thinned and are
of the age class between 20 and 40 years. Given the predominance of pine and other coniferous species,
pine has been chosen to represent the forest type to be modelled. Four age group models were prepared
and project silviculture activity is allocated as follows (Table 2 ):

8.          In the short nm, thinming the inadequately tdinned young forests (20-37years) would lead
to an increase in production of smallwood suitable mainly for fuelwood and pulpwood. However, larger
profit margins are derived from higher sawlog and veneerlog volume, generated in the medium and long
term (i.e., years 40 to 100). The incremental gross value of the increased volume of the hight value
timber - sawlogs and veneerlogs - is about US$ 4 bfllion over the stand rotation (100 years: harvesting
would be between 60 to 80 years after project implementation). These incremental benefits are not
captred in the calculation of the ERR because the discounting effect for the time value of the cash flows
reduces all flows after the 25th project year to a minimal impact on the ERR (para. 11).
9.           A substantial proportion of the pulp- and fuelwood is currenly either wasted or utilized
for low value wood products. An indication of the current lack of demand for fuelwood is reflected by
some rayons failure to take delivery of all of their current allocation of fuelwood. The economic decline
has dried up traditional markets (namely Russia) for Belarus pulpwood, and prospects for export have
been heavily impacted on by both the present world recession and the recent rise in Russian rail rates.
10.          The ERR analysis for the wood benefits from intensive silvicultue assume smallwood
produced by the project is constant, but consumed at various levels, and the consumption level is valued
at the oil equivalent price (Table 4). The low case scenario is a break even exercise to achieve a 0%
ERR; the base case output level achieves the cost of borrowing (15%) and the high case is arbitrarily

II.          The other silviculture revenue components, sawlogs and veneerlogs have been valued at
their export parity prices. This is in line with the expectation that Belarus will in the medium term exports
logs as its proportion of mature forests will have increased to sustain both domestic demand and exports.
However, the incremental benefit flows from these two products occur well beyond the significant time
stream of the project and have only a 1% point increase on the ERR, under any of the scenarios.2

carried out by estimating the value of machinery operating cost savings, by comparison with conducting
thinnings using the existing heavy ex-USSR equipment and its associated work organization system,
13.          Light mechanizon will be introduced for the intensive silviculture areas during the first
three years of the project and is expected to reap benefits over the first five to eight years, which is the
lifetime of the machinery. Returns to mechanization in thinning are dependent upon the efficiencies
(theoretically) achievable through reorganizing the work force around a changed technology (chainsaws
and light mecanization). The technology itself saves in both capital and labor, but the gains will only

and the variable costs by US$ 9.70/r3. In order to ensure that equipment associated with light

15.          Wben both streams of benefits are evaluated against the silviculture equipment investnent,
the combined ERR for the base case scenario is 40%.
Spare Parts
16.          The investment costs of harvesting under the present system was contrasted to a number
of alternatives, and repair of the existing machinery through a pare parts program was found to provide
the highest return even though a compromise is involved. This compromise is a delay in changing over
to lighter machinery with even lower unit operating costs, and a delay in the reorganization of harvestng
to the assortment method. The benefits from harvesting machinery repair are estimated to occur over
the first six years of the project (the machinery is introduced during the first two years of the project).
Incremental benefits were derived by neting the average costs of the repaired machinery with the
marginal costs of the in-use machinery, incorporating the US$3.1 million investment stream. The ERR
is 24% (Table 9.7).
Forest Fires Magent
17.          Numerous forest fires take place in Belarus every year and many of these can be better
controlled, if not avoided, with an adequate system of protection and fire fighting practices. The project
will provide both the equipment and the management know-how to reduce the occurrence and the impact
of the fires, which, at an average size of 1.25 ha is fairly small by western standards. On average some
2,858 hectares of forest are destroyed due to fire. In the drought year of 1992, when available resources



ANNEX9
page 6 of 22
for fire-fighting were reduced, some 22,148 hectares were burnt, due in part to both a large ntmber of
fire starts (7,444, versus an average amnual of about 2,550), and a more than doubling in the average size
of burn (2.98 ha, versus the average 1.25 ha). It is this evident strain on resources that project
interventions are planned to relieve.
18.          The valuations of benefits from fire prevention stem from the reduced size of average fire
(it is assumed that little can be done about the number of fire starts). The reduction in average fire size
(in hectares) gives rise to a flow of benefits to the economy over time; these benefits being approximated
by the value of the wood which might be harvested. The commercial value of a forest stand, assuming
a 4% discount rate over the life of the stand, approximates US$ 1,256/ha (Table 9.8). Such a stand is
considered to have an equivalent enviromnental value when one factors MinFor's assessment of benefits
to the economy from a stand of forest, in the following order of priority:
-     biodiversity protection and enhancement
-     soil, water and fragile eco-systems conservation
-     carbon sequestration
-     national landscape, the forest heritage, recreation and tourism
-     commercial timber production
-     wildlife and other forest products production.
19.          The benefits exclude the threat posed by fires in the Chernobyl-affected 'red forests',
where the smoke plume could furhffer spread nuclear conutination, and where fire-fighting presents even
higher health and life-risks to the fire-fighters. These "red forests" are in oblasts (e.g., Gomel and
Mogliev) with above-average incidence of forest fires. When adjusting notionally for environmental
benefits and when considering the public health aspects of reduced nisk of radionucide contamnation,
an additional US$ 1,256/ha is considered as a reasonable assessment of the value of these environmental
factors, the intrinsic value of the forest resources saved and the health care costs avoided. The mission
has assessed that a 30% reduction in average annual area burned will result from the project's investment
in fire protection. At a value of US$ 2,512/ha for both commercial and environmental value saved, and
assuming a continuation of the trend of mber of fires started over the 1982 - 1992 period, the fire
investment component generates an ERR of approximately 9%.
Forest Mana        Information System WNW
20.          The FMIS project component is designed to improve the flow of information and enb3nce
the prompt availability of forestry data base. This will facilitate improved monitoring and planning of
forestry operations. The usefulness of the FMIS will be appreciated as Belarus' command economy
transforming to a market economy and more accurate data will be requested by private or non private
commercial operators. Although quantifying the benefits under this component is difficult, a conservative
estimate of at least 5 percent savings on the forestry administradon costs has been assumed to be realized
as the result of improved management information. Assuming a total silviculture budget of Rb 17 billion
for 1993 in June 1993 prices and exchange rate, annual savings at full development would be of the order
of Rb 850 million (US$ 0.68 million), generating an ERR of 22% (Table 9.9).

page 7 of 22
Seed Processing and Sorage
21.          The project proposes to establish a network of seed selecting, processing and storage
facilites in order to mamize the production of high quality seed coming from seed orchards and from
selected trees. The benefits from improved seed are as follows:
(a)   improved seed genrination rates kless seedig per seedling produced);
(b)   improved seedling quality Qower seedling rejection quantity - higher yield of
transplantable seedlings per ton of seed);
(c)   improved seedling survival (lower planting density and lower level of fill-in planting);
(d)   faster growth (shorter rotation cycle, earlier close of canopy); and
(e)    more vigorous growth (higher volume per tree, less tree stems required per hectare).
22.          These benefits are reflected in the stand models (Anm  10; Table 10.6 & 10.7) for with
and without improved seed, as follows:
(a)    The WithoWt case, with intensified silviculture (as recommended under this project) has
a seedling density of 7,0001ha. Total wood volume produced to maturity is shown in
Table 6 below;
(b) The With case, with intensified silviculture (as recommended under this project) has a
seedling density of 3,500/ha. Total wood volume produced to maturity is shown in
Table 9 below. Aside from higher yields of higher value wood (sawlogs, veneer)
associated with this case, the maintenance costs are lower.

23.          The stand model assumes a 20 percent higher cost of seedlings for improved genetic stock
than for un-selected stock, reflecing only the production costs of these seedlings, and not any recovery
of the capital investment in the seed facility. The Net Benefit stream (Table 9.10) is therefore positive.
The NPV of these incremental Net Benefits from the total rotation (i.e., to year 100), assuming the cost
of capital to the economy estimated at 15%, can be used to reflect the intrinsic value of the genetic
material of the seeds and the value of their collection and storage. Using an economic price for labor
at 87% of the market price (i.e. SCF of labor), the NPV's for incremental benefits from I ha. of
gedcally improved stock is US $105 in economic terms (Table 9.10).

C. Composite Retumn
28.            The composite ERR for the total project is 33 %. (Table 9.12) This composite average
includes those Project components having quantifiable returns; namely intensified silviculture, thinnings
mechanization, spare parts, fire management, the seed facility and benefit from the PMIS.
D. Sensitivity Analysis
29.           The critcal factors in the project are demand for fuelwood and pulpwood and
corresponding by correct pricing policy, and the success or failure in establishing adequate incentive
systems (including pricing) for the realization of expected gains in productivity in thimning and harvesting.
Investment and operational costs are incidental to the project relative the sensitivity of fuelwood and
pulpwood prices assumed. The returns are far less sensitive to increasing operating costs of silviculture
than to the demand for fuelwood. The ERR is also not sensitive to longer term benefits from the sawlogs
and veneerlogs because the time value discount minimizes their value.
30.           The price of fuelwood in the short and medium run (i.e., the course of the project) is in
tum dependent upon policy. Whether the fuel import parity price, US$ 11.23 per cubic meter, which
is roughly twelve times the calculated "economic " price of the smallest diameter wood (US$ 0.89/m3p,
is appropriate depends largely upon: (a) anticipated increased energy prices passed on to industial and
household consumers, encouraging substitution of fuelwood for gas, oil and electricity; (b) industrial
energy consumers (especially in the forestry-related sectors) switching over to wood-fueled technology,
and (c) the extent in the long run that municipalities and indus;tres in general be given aid and technical
support in convertig Heat-only Boilers) and thermal plants over to fuelwood technology.
4.     This composite  tum is relatively highe than the overall retuns of two forest projects presented to the
Board in 1993 - the Poland Foresty Development Support Project (Report No. 11318-POL) with an
overall ERR of 18%, and the Republic of Tunisia Second Foresry Development Project ( Report No.
11618-TUN) with an overal ERR of 13%. The Poland project is environment and social-economically
oriented. This explains the relatively higher unit invent costs (compared to the Belarus project) for the
volume of (short-term) thinningsproductionobtained. The Tunisia project's focus is largely environmental;
commercial benefits are a by-product of its implementation.
S.     US$ 0.89/m3 was derived as a function of the economic price of wood not taldng into account fuel
substution value, by detemining a derived economic value of the four wood components - the smallest
diameter wood ("fuelwood"), pulpwood, sawlogs and veneer based on an established price proportion
among these 4 products and to achieve a 4% economic return on the silviculture investment expended.



ANNEX 9
page 10 of 22
31.          If energy price hikes are in large part passed along to consumers, then it may be
anticipated that conversion to fuelwood use will be made at heating plants at a number of levels within
the economy, and that the scarcity value of fuelwood will be approximately US $11.23Imn, as the
fuelwood is replacing imported oil, gas and electricity. The project can encourage this demand by aiding
in the conversion to fuelwood use in the forestry-related industrial sector, which is both a substantial user
of energy and a convenient source of wood for fuel.
32.          Nonetheless, there are quite good reasons for the scarcity value of fuelwood and
pulpwood to achieve a price level of US$ 11.231/i3. The price increases are certain to be imposed at the
border; they are of such an order of magnitude as to make subsidization highly improbable; the
technology for conversion to fuelwood use is simple, weii-known, and inexpensive; the project has a
component to hasten the conversion to fuelwood; and project planning for the energy sector will doubtless
build upon this analysis in determining investment priorities.
